LIVE: IMF Managing Director speaks ahead of the IMF/World Bank spring meetings

Reuters(4.2M) ·75:45 ·578 views · 2026-04-09
Kristalina Georgieva (Managing Director, IMF)
The global economy faces a severe, asymmetric supply shock driven by Middle East conflict, resulting in significant energy disruptions, slowed growth, and heightened food insecurity.
TL;DR The global economy faces a severe, asymmetric supply shock driven by Middle East conflict, resulting in significant energy disruptions, slowed growth, and heightened food insecurity. Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva warns that while the world remains resilient,...

TL;DR

The global economy faces a severe, asymmetric supply shock driven by Middle East conflict, resulting in significant energy disruptions, slowed growth, and heightened food insecurity. Managing Director Kristalina Georgieva warns that while the world remains resilient, countries must rebuild "fiscal space" and focus on productivity-enhancing structural reforms to survive frequent, overlapping shocks.

The Nature and Impact of the Supply Shock

  • Energy and Logistics Disruptions: The conflict has resulted in a 13% cut to global daily oil flow and a 20% cut to LNG flow.
      • Price Volatility: Brent crude peaked at $120 per barrel; while prices have receded, they remain significantly higher than pre-war levels.
      • Infrastructure Scarring: Qatar’s Ras Laffan complex (producing 93% of Gulf LNG) suffered a direct hit and may take three to five years to restore to full capacity.
      • Shipping Stagnation: Transit through the Red Sea’s Bab el-Mandeb remains stuck at approximately half of 2023 levels.
  • Secondary Ripple Effects:
      • Food Insecurity: Higher transport costs and fertilizer prices have pushed an additional 45 million people into hunger, bringing the global total to over 360 million.
      • Industrial Dependencies: Supply chain disruptions are affecting critical materials like helium (for silicon chips/MRIs), sulfur, and naphtha.

Economic Outlook and Growth Scenarios

  • Downgraded Projections: The IMF’s forthcoming World Economic Outlook will present a range of scenarios; even the most optimistic case expects lower growth than previously projected due to infrastructure damage and loss of confidence.
  • The Three Channels of Impact:
      • Price Impact: Higher input costs lift inflation and reduce demand via "brute force."
      • Inflation Expectations: Short-run expectations are shifting higher, particularly in the US and Euro area, though long-term anchors remain steady.
      • Financial Conditions: Emerging market bond spreads have widened and the dollar has appreciated, tightening global financial conditions.
  • Vulnerability Quadrant: Sub-Saharan Africa and Pacific Island nations are the most vulnerable, facing high import costs with very little sovereign credit or policy space to buffer the shock.

Policy Recommendations: "Do No Harm"

  • Fiscal Responsibility: Georgieva warns against "driving with one foot on the gas and one on the brake."
      • Avoid Broad Subsidies: Fiscal authorities must reject untargeted tax cuts or energy subsidies that mute price signals.
      • Rebuilding Space: Public debt is significantly higher than 20 years ago; most countries must decisively rebuild fiscal buffers as interest payments consume larger shares of revenue.
  • Monetary Policy Calibration:
      • Watch and Wait: Central banks should remain data-dependent with a bias toward action only if inflation expectations become unanchored.
      • Avoid Premature Tightening: Unnecessary rate hikes could unnecessarily stifle growth and turn a supply shock into a demand shock.
  • Structural Reforms: Countries are urged to remove "red tape" and obstacles to innovation. Private sector adaptability is cited as the primary driver of recent global resilience.

The Evolving Role of the IMF and Global Shifts

  • The AI Tsunami: AI could provide a 0.1% to 0.8% boost to global productivity, potentially lifting growth above pre-pandemic trajectories. However, 60% of jobs in advanced economies will be affected, necessitating careful labor market management.
  • Firepower and Funding:
      • 16th Quota Review: The IMF is seeking US Congressional approval for a quota increase to ensure "firepower" against future crises.
      • Financial Model: Georgieva noted the IMF paid the US $3.1 billion in interest over the last two years, highlighting its role as a "savings bank" rather than a cost to taxpayers.
      • Anticipated Demand: The IMF expects demand for balance of payment support to rise by $20–$50 billion in the near term.
  • Weaponization of Interdependence: In a fragmented world with multiple centers of gravity, the IMF stresses that autarky is impossible; countries must instead find "expanded value propositions" where mutual gains are explicitly mapped.
"Fiscal policy absolutely everywhere has to be very careful not to put in place unaffordable measures... It would be like driving with one foot on the accelerator and the other one on the brake. Not good." — Kristalina Georgieva
"I am absolutely in the camp that believes that AI brings tremendous productivity enhancement potential... But, we also at the Fund are mindful of the risks for labor dislocation. We actually see in advanced economies 60 percent of jobs being affected by AI. This is like a tsunami hitting the labor market." — Kristalina Georgieva
"One thing that is not possible is to pull yourself out of any interdependence and say 'I’m gonna do it all on my own.' Even the largest economies cannot do that." — Kristalina Georgieva
Please welcome to the stage, President of the Council on Foreign Relations, Michael Froman. Good morning, everybody. My name is Mike Froman. I'm President of the Council on Foreign Relations. And we are delighted to be collaborating with the International Monetary Fund on the eve of the 2026 Spring Meetings. It's my job and my great honor this morning to introduce the Managing Director, Dr. Kristalina Georgieva, to help us understand what will be happening next week and give us an economic outlook, particularly with reference to what's going on in Iran and the global economy. We'll have 20 minutes of her thoughts and then we'll open it up to questions, first from me and then from a couple members of our audience. She truly needs no introduction, particularly in this hall. But she's been the Managing Director since 2019. She was re-elected in 2024. Before that, as you know, she was CEO of the World Bank from 2017 to 2019, where she secured a 13 billion dollar capital increase for the bank at a very difficult time. She is one of the most consequential economic leaders in recent history. And it's a great honor to call her a friend as well. Please welcome the Managing Director. Thank you, Mike. A very good morning to all. Good morning. A resilient world economy is being tested again by a now post-war in the Middle East. The conflict has caused considerable hardship in the region and around the globe. My heart goes out to all people affected by this war and all wars. Next week we would welcome ministers and central bank governors to our Spring Meetings, and our focus will be on how best to weather this latest shock and ease the pain on economies and on people. This requires understanding the nature of the shock, the channels through which it affects the economy, the size of the impact, and the policies that can mitigate it. So what hit us? A supply shock that is large, with the world's daily oil flow cut by some 13 percent, and its LNG flow by some 20 percent. It is global, with all of us now paying more for energy, and with supply chains disrupted around the world. And it is asymmetric, with its impact depending on proximity to the conflict, whether you are an energy exporter or importer, and your policy space. As always, a negative supply shock pushes prices up. As a point of reference, Brent jumped from $72 per barrel on the eve of hostilities to a peak of $120. Thankfully oil prices have fallen, but they remain much higher than before the war, and many countries are paying a high premium for access to precious supplies. Spare a thought for the Pacific island nations, at the end of a long supply chain, wondering if fuel still reaches them in the wake of such severe disruption. The supply interruptions have had, and will, for some time, continue to have ripple effects. Such as: oil refineries disruptions, disruptions because of the need to maintain minimum flow rates, with warning lights flashing in many far-flung places. Shortages of refined products, including diesel and jet fuel. They disturb transportation, trade, tourism, in a world that is more interconnected than ever. Food insecurity is pushing another 45 million people into hunger given the transport restriction problems, taking the total number of people in hunger to over 360 million, with a problem that is potentially going to get worse over time because of higher fertilizer prices. And supply chain disruptions given industrial dependencies on sulfur, on helium for silicon chip making and MRI imaging, and naphtha for plastics. The second question is how can this shock play out? Through three main channels. First, the price impact and supply shortages. Higher prices for key inputs feed into many consumer goods, lifting inflation. This, coupled with shortages, reduces demand by a brute force. Second channel: inflation expectations. These can break anchor and ignite a costly inflation process. Been there, seen it. Here is the distribution of near-term inflation forecasts for the US. Notice how the curve has moved to the right, indicating higher short-run inflation expectations. And here is the curve for the euro area. It also moves to the right, and also it widens, indicating higher uncertainty. Now there is some good news. Fortunately, long-term expectations have not budged. Very good and very important. Third channel: financial conditions. From a highly supportive starting point, they tightened in an orderly manner. Emerging market bond spreads widened substantially. Equity prices have adjusted, and the dollar appreciated. We have been here before in the 70s, and earlier this decade. And we know eventually a significant part of the shock will dissipate, leaving us in a new equilibrium. Supply recovers, demand adjusts, new capacity comes on stream, energy efficiency rises. As a proof, just appreciate how the world has become progressively less energy intensive since the 80s. And that picture what you see on the top, this cushions the shock we face today. Renewable energy increased its share. Yet oil remains our number one fuel. As the world responds, it is important that we maintain our collective quest for energy efficiency and energy diversification. Different countries have different paths to energy security, but all must strive for it. Let me move to the third question. How large is the growth impact? The answer very much depends on whether the ceasefire holds and leads to lasting peace. One. And two, how much damage the war leaves in its wake. Given the uncertainties, our World Economic Outlook to be published next week—you can see Pierre-Olivier Gourinchas here on the first row, sitting comfortably because the work is done—it would present a range of scenarios going from a relatively swift normalization, which would be of course the best option, to a middle scenario, to one where oil and gas prices stay much higher for much longer, and second-round effects take hold. All these scenarios start from a situation where strong AI and tech investment, supportive financial conditions, and other factors were driving considerable momentum in the world economy. In fact, had it not been for this shock, the world would have been in a such a much better place than we would have been upgrading our growth projections. But now, even in our most hopeful scenario, we would have a downgrade. Why? Because of significant infrastructure damage, supply disruptions, losses of confidence, and other scarring effects. They are already baked in. Take Qatar's Ras Laffan complex. A tremendously important example of strategic investment done right. Producer of 93 percent of the Gulf's LNG, some 80 percent of it going to Asia Pacific, a region that now endures serious fuel shortages. Ras Laffan has essentially been shut since March 2nd. It took a direct hit on March 19, and could take three to five years to restore to full capacity. Even in the best case, there will be no neat and clean return to the status quo ante. Another relevant fact: note how ship passages through Bab el-Mandeb on the Red Sea have never quite recovered from the devastating disruptions there. They remain stuck at about half of their 2023 level. So the reality is we don't truly know what the future holds for transits through the Strait of Hormuz, or for that matter, for the full recovery of regional air traffic. What we do know is that growth will be slower. Even if the new peace is durable. And we also know there are significant variations of the impact of this shock around the world, and I really want to dwell on this point. Countries able to export oil and gas undisturbed are the least affected. In contrast, countries directly disturbed by the war, including oil and gas exporters who suffer the blockade, and countries relying on imported oil and gas, bear the brunt of the impact. How bad it will be depends in no small measure on how much policy space countries have, how much buffering they can put in action. And that includes also how much they have as oil and gas reserves given the five-week gap we have seen in tanker traffic from the Gulf. So let me walk you through a few key charts to illustrate three points of differentiation. First, let's separate the world into oil importers, on the left—sorry, let me turn this way—on the left, and oil exporters on the right. As the pile up of dots on the left shows, over 80 percent of countries are oil importers. Second, let's highlight countries directly hit by this war. And notice how this disproportionately falls on major oil exporters. Although the red dots on the left remind us of the war's toll on regional non-oil economies as well. Third, and I want us to pay attention to this. Let's add vertical scale for countries' sovereign credit rating, as proxy for policy space. The bottom left quadrant is where we find vulnerable oil importers. Let's color Sub-Saharan Africa in yellow. And small island nations in orange. Notice how these two sets of countries largely fill that quadrant of vulnerability. And they will be very much a focus of our discussion next week. Now, given that oil is a global commodity, even oil exporters far from the affected region, and enjoying terms of trade gains, feel the impact of costlier oil. So let me... this is the picture, this is where we are coming from. Let's move to the last and most important question. What should countries do? And I will start with a word of caution up front. This being a classic negative supply shock, demand adjustment is unavoidable. To put it in non-technical language, we cannot go through it without some pain. Policymakers can help to reduce this pain in multiple ways. And certainly one way in which they can help is please don't make matters worse. So I appeal to all countries to reject go-it-alone actions. Export controls, price controls, and so on that can further upset global conditions. Don't pour gasoline on the fire. You need this gasoline to drive your cars. The challenge will be to detect if and when changing conditions take us from one state of the world to another. For now, there is value in waiting and watching, with central banks stressing their commitment to price stability, but otherwise staying on hold, with a stronger bias to action if credibility is in question. Fiscal authorities should provide targeted and temporary support to the vulnerable, aligned with their medium-term fiscal frameworks. Next, if inflation expectations threaten to break anchor and ignite a costly inflation spiral, then central banks should step in firmly with rate hikes. Fiscal support should remain targeted and temporary. Rate hikes of course would further dampen growth. That's how they work, and that is the right price to pay for price stability. Finally, in a severe tightening of financial conditions, they add a negative demand shock to the supply shock. And then monetary policy returns to a delicate balancing act, while fiscal policy, if and only if there is fiscal space, switches to well-calibrated demand support. So let's see what is actually happening out there. In monetary policy, what are markets telling us? That they are expecting major central banks to tighten their policy stance. Here we see four key market-implied policy rate paths, each showing an upward shift. In energy policy, we see many countries putting in place emergency conservation measures. From general campaigns, to limits on private vehicle use, to remote work. These and other steps are well documented by the International Energy Agency. By the way, this is the reason we formed a partnership—the World Bank, International Energy Agency and the Fund—where we concentrate on the macro side. And what this graph tells us is that, in terms of economic support measures, quite a number of countries are doing the right thing. They are going with targeted measures. They are shaping them to be temporary. But then we also have broad-based measures, those that are in red, and, while we haven't done a detailed analysis, it does worry me that there is so much red on this slide. So let me move to looking into what is the step we need to take on the fiscal policy front. We have to avoid untargeted tax cuts. We have to avoid energy subsidies that are not well targeted. And I cannot stress enough, we have to do it recognizing how limited fiscal space we have. We will point out that measures that mute the price signal, they also mute the necessary demand response, resulting in higher global energy prices. And we will work with countries to make sure that, as the situation evolves, they have the agility to correct their policy stance appropriately. So, let me also look at another part of reality. What is happening on the financial front. Already the world has seen benchmark yield curves rising, driving up the cost of debt. Adding deficit-financed stimulus to this mix at this moment would increase the burden on monetary policy and amplify such shifts. It would be like driving with one foot on the accelerator and the other one on the brake. Not good. As we will flag in our forthcoming Fiscal Monitor, the world does have a fiscal space problem. Public debt is generally much higher than 20 years ago, including in most G20 countries. Reflecting widespread neglect of fiscal consolidation in the periods when conditions permitted it. As a result, interest payments are rising as a share of revenues at all income levels. And the implication is clear. All countries must deploy their fiscal resources responsibly. And most must move decisively to rebuilding fiscal space after this shock. And I cannot emphasize this enough. And let me say a word about financial sector policies. As our Global Financial Stability Report, Tobias Adrian is right here, would insist, it is essential that financial regulators and supervisors are alert, nimble, and responsive to a fluid situation. Financial conditions have been highly accommodative for some time. Spurred by tech optimism, and new financial intermediaries, many of them non-banks. While this has lifted growth, it also creates risks of reversal. If investors were to start worrying about energy insecurity holding back the growth of AI, given AI's huge energy needs, then we could find ourselves in a spot of trouble. Micro and macroprudential policies must work to reduce financial stability risks and ensure a resilient system. With that, I want to stress the most important lesson of all. Good policies make a difference. There are forces countries cannot control. But they do have authority over their own policies and institutions. Take heed. The strength and agility of your fundamentals is your best defense when shocks come. And come they will. And as you deal with the long tail of the current shock, do not forget to steer the great global transformations in technology, in demographics, in geopolitics, in trade, in climate, to build a better future. Your structural and regulatory policy choices underpin productivity and long-run growth, and growth potential matters enormously for stability. You grow more, all other things equal, you are in a better place. For us at the IMF, supporting you to build strong policies, to build strong institutions—this is our raison d'être. This is why we exist. And as the firefighter, we are here for you when a crisis hits. Once more, let's take a look at the vulnerable oil importing countries. Those that are in the left quadrant here at the bottom. We color in blue all countries with IMF-supported programs. What you notice? There are many. And we can scale up these programs if needed to be sure to help countries. And more programs will come. Given the spillovers from the war, we expect near-term demand for IMF balance of payment support to rise by somewhere between 20 and 50 billion dollars, with the lower bound prevailing if ceasefire holds. Two points worth noting here. One, this range would have been much higher if not for the sound policymaking of many emerging market economies. Including some of the largest ones over the decade. So those in the audience from emerging market economies that have built strong fundamentals, strong buffers, bravo! Thank you. And two, we are well resourced to meet this shock. So yes, our 191 member countries can count on us to support them with financing if needed. And they can count on us to bring them together to find a path forward through the fog of uncertainty. This is what next week will be about. Thank you. And let us hope for lasting peace in the Middle East and everywhere. Because war takes away everything we work for. Thank you. Well that was terrific. Thank you very much. And I was just complimenting both the graphics that went along with the speech and this amazing wall. I mean what a, what a terrific asset to have here to explain, to explain all of these policies. Kristalina, you've, you've walked us through and as you said, a lot depends on whether the ceasefire is fully implemented and how long it lasts, whether there's ultimately a solution that comes out of the Middle East. You've got these finance ministers and central bank governors arriving next week. How do you advise them about how to prepare when there's so much uncertainty about the basics that are going to drive the economic challenges that they're likely to face? Well the most important message we have for our members is get your house in good order. When this shock dissipates, there will be another one to come. So building strong, resilient economies. Paying attention to longer-term transformational forces. Investing in productivity enhancing measures. Above all, taking steps that cost no money but deliver economic results. For example, we have seen in the previous shock—we would see it in this shock—that private sector is more agile, more adaptable. One of the reasons the world economy has shown so much resilience is because of that private sector adaptability. Well, remove red tape, remove obstacles to innovation, create conditions for capital to take measured risk. And on that basis, lift up productivity. I am very mindful that this is easy to say and hard to do. This is why our teams have been working on what are structural policies that can support productivity enhancement and growth. And then on a country by country specific basis, work with governments for these policies to take hold. We also would be advising them, work together. We are... the world is interconnected. Yes, it is more fragmented. Yes, there are more tensions. But there is so much potential in working together within regions, across the globe. And the meetings are a great way of nurturing this spirit of cooperation. And I want to thank—I see in the audience many, many of our executive directors. I want to thank them for all the work they do to prepare their governors for that kind of inclusive discussion. So do no harm, do what costs no money decisively, and work together. There's a phrase that's common in this city, which is don't let a crisis go to waste. Do you think governments have a natural tendency towards complacency, even because some of these structural reforms are quite difficult? Do you think there is a sufficient sense of crisis to get them to move along this agenda? I have seen countries taking advantage of a crisis for tremendous transformation. And I want to give a shout out to countries like Greece, Ireland, Portugal. They were in a massive crisis, Eurozone crisis. I am very grateful to our teams at the IMF for the work the IMF did with these countries, and look at them now. Best performing economies in Europe. How did they do it? You buckle up, you do the tough stuff, you bring people with you. And the fact that today the President of the Eurogroup is the Greek Finance Minister makes that point. Political will, commitment, and ability to do tough reforms pays off. So more of this. Less of this. You made the point that in the context of this supply shock, we're likely to see price increases. We could see a breakout of inflation pushing central banks to raise rates precisely at the time that their fiscal authorities are going to be trying to ensure that there isn't a collapse of demand and slipping into a recession. How do you advise again these countries on the prioritization, the sequencing, and ensuring that they don't as you say, put one foot on the gas and one foot on the brake at the same time? What is very important to recognize is that, different from COVID when all countries were in the same boat, we had demand and supply collapsing simultaneously, and governments, monetary authorities, fiscal authorities, they had to do all the same thing: monetary policy accommodation, fiscal policy accommodation. And they had fiscal space, many of them. They had policy space to do it. Now it's different. Now different countries are in a different place in terms of inflation. You look at Asia for example; many countries in Asia have not been experiencing inflationary pressures. They may see some of it, but they're in a different place. And then you go to countries where inflation has not yet been defeated from the previous round. So very important for monetary policy to be calibrated to country specific circumstances. Fiscal policy. Unfortunately, we have a fiscal space problem. Almost universally. We have seen debt levels going up with interest rates higher—I showed that on the screen—it is eating from government budgets a bigger and bigger chunk. So fiscal policy absolutely everywhere has to be very careful not to put in place unaffordable measures. And this is why I was showing you the red on this screen. I am proud of the countries that had the bravery to really focus on targeted and temporary measures. And I think unfortunately here there is no easy way around. What should countries also think of? How can they lift up their growth potential? Because the problem of high debt can only be melted away with high growth. And high growth would only come with high productivity. You look at the United States where productivity has been growing robustly, and growth has been quite strong. Comes this shock, it is not as dramatic for the US economy. I think that there is a lot to be learned from the US in terms of creating innovation environment, in terms of deep capital markets, and risk-taking attitude. I also think that, in the US now with deregulation, there would be more, more 'woof' in the economy. But be careful. Don't deregulate to a point in which from asset that turns into a liability. So are you in that camp that believes that the AI-induced potential productivity increases is going to save us from all of our other economic problems? I am absolutely in the camp that believes that AI brings tremendous productivity enhancement potential. Our staff has done an analysis and it says gain in productivity can be between 0.1 and 0.8 percent, actually productivity turning into growth impact. 0.8 is significant, massive. Even if we are at midpoint 0.4, this would lift global growth above the pre-pandemic trajectory. Now, could be even more. But we also at the Fund are mindful of the risks for labor dislocation and labor market disruption. We actually see in advanced economies 60 percent, six zero, 60 percent of jobs being affected by AI over the next years. This is like a tsunami hitting the, the labor market. And the issue is many of these jobs would be enhanced, they would be more productive, but there would be jobs that would be also eliminated. And we need to think hard, and then work with private sector, how do we steer this process more consciously. And here is my problem. There are so many topics that hit us. I mean, I think that the world has developed an attention deficit disorder. That we can't really stay on one hugely important issue which is how to gather the benefits of AI and mitigate the risks consistently. You know, let me ask the audience. How many of you are optimists about AI? How many of you are pessimists about AI? So here we go. The audience is with us. Thank you. You know the IMF, indeed the whole Bretton Woods system in many respects was built on the supposition about the benefits of interdependence. And in that, we look back over the last several years, we've seen the weaponization of interdependence, whether it's the financial system, supply chains, or now with the Strait of Hormuz, the weaponization of geography. What lessons do you think countries might take from these developments about their approach going forward? Are they likely to be more autarkic? Are they likely to pull back from interdependence? And what does that mean for global growth? Well the, the world has changed quite significantly and we have to recognize the depth and breadth of this change, and then lean forward into the world of today and tomorrow rather than trying to somehow hang to the world of yesterday. And one of the big changes is a world of multiple centers of gravity. Different alliances—some of these alliances are more stable, some are less stable, some are economic, some are geopolitical. And that makes it more complicated for countries to figure out how to go about their dependency on relations with others. But let me say two principles are in play. The first principle is we are interconnected. So that, one thing that is not possible is to pull yourself out of any interdependence and say I'm gonna do it all on my own. Even the largest economies cannot do that. And of course if you are a smaller open economy, it's absolutely not possible. So you have to work on interdependence aspects. And the second one is, be objective in answering not only the question what is in for me, but also the question what is in for the other guy. And then recognize that there has to be expanded value proposition for everybody. If it is something in which one gains and the other one loses, I guarantee you it is not going to last very long. And this is where international organizations have a responsibility to objectively map out strategic advantages, disadvantages, and then help our membership to find pathways of mutually beneficial engagements. How do we do that? By being candid, by being honest in our analysis, not pretend that a problem doesn't exist, and then accept that sometimes some of our members when we present this analysis, they may be a bit, kind of rattled by it, and find a way to communicate the outcome of analysis in a way that countries would accept it. And that is very important for us to recognize that not always it is about being right, it is also about being measured, and timing, engagement, doing it in a way that is respectful of others. So a lot to learn. Everybody has to learn to live in this new world and IMF is no exception. And I'll tell you we have wonderful people at the Fund that are on this learning journey every day. And what do we bring? Transmission line of knowledge. We learn from different countries, we pass this knowledge around. And with these changes that you were talking about, and including disruption of the global trading system, greater fragmentation, how has the approach or the theory of the case for the IMF changed? Has it evolved over the last 10 or 15 years in terms of the kinds of advice it's giving countries? So yes, of course, the world changes, the IMF changes as well. I would say the two very significant systemic changes I have seen in my time and I have seen it in, when Christine Lagarde was here being undertaken are one, recognizing that the acceptability of reforms, the political economy of reforms matters. And that we need to understand how our policy measures affect people. That ultimately all we do is for people. And that element of empathy—sometimes reforms are very painful, understanding that, and then thinking of mitigating factors to increase the acceptability of reforms. To improve engagement within society. And I see that really changed. I see it time and again when I engage with our members. And the second big change that has happened for the Fund is that we now see that our headquarter base and our in-field presence, they need to work holistically. We are mostly headquarter-based institution. We have about 4,000 staff; 3,000 are here, 1,000 out in the field. But how we work with our training institutes, how we work with our regional offices, with our local presence, so we have that constant transmission of knowledge up and down that makes our ability to help countries more durable. I think also the Fund has proven that we are indispensable in a world of frequent shocks. There is no other institution that can, as the Fund, step in forcefully, protect our members, and protect not only for the benefit of the individual member, but also because of the spillover risks. Because trouble travels. If one part of the world is disturbed and vulnerable, that vulnerability affects others. And that I can say with, from the bottom of my heart, I am so grateful to each and every one of the staff of the Fund for what we do. To be that anchor of stability in a world that has more trouble. Terrific. We'll, we'll take a few questions from the audience. Let me first, I see Heidi Crebo-Rediker. A microphone is on its way. First of all, thank you so much, Managing Director and Mike. So in President Trump's FY 26 budget, he called on Congress to approve the general review of quotas for the 16th review. So this is something that would double the quota for the IMF, and it would keep the US veto. Is this something that—I think do you have confidence that this will, that Congress will act in a timely fashion given all of the challenges you just outlined? And if not, what are the consequences for your firepower and your ability to deal with that lower quadrant that you just referred to? Thank you. Let me express my, my heartfelt gratitude to the administration for putting the 16th quota review for approval with Congress and to everybody who has worked on that in US Treasury, but also on the Hill. We have had many engagements to make sure that people understand why this is a good thing for the world and for the United States. I found in my visits on the Hill that, for example, not everybody understood the financial model of the Fund. We are like a savings bank. We have pledges of money, let's call them deposits, of our members. We lend, and then we pay back with interest. We are not like other institutions that need congressional appropriation. Last two years we paid the US 3.1 billion in interest on the money we, we, generously the US allowed us to lend. So that, I think is the simple answer: is this a burden for the US. But then comes the benefit. And of course it is a high benefit for the US to have the IMF surveying all countries, providing to everybody from one point of entry a picture of the economic health of the world and each region and each country, and then of course to come as a rescuer very often to economies that are significant, including for the US. So I am fairly optimistic that given the value we offer, that would lead to, to approval. And we would make every effort to show and answer questions related to the appropriation to answer your question would it happen in a, in a timely manner. I, you know it is in the budget, so we will work to get it to happen. But to give comfort to the audience, we have firing power. We need this increase. Why we need it? 2026 ZKast #63 - NVIDIA GTC: How Networking is Powering the AI Revolution | What’s New in Networking

Zeus Kerravala(28.0K) ·20:29 ·6.1K views · 2026-04-09
Zeus Kerravala (Host, ZKast)
The networking fabric is no longer a background player but has become as critical as the GPU itself, with NVIDIA driving massive bandwidth breakthroughs and architectural shifts—including "context-aware" storage and AI-powered mobile networks—to sustain the next generation of AI scale.
TL;DR The networking fabric is no longer a background player but has become as critical as the GPU itself, with NVIDIA driving massive bandwidth breakthroughs and architectural shifts—including "context-aware" storage and AI-powered mobile networks—to sustain the next generation...

TL;DR

The networking fabric is no longer a background player but has become as critical as the GPU itself, with NVIDIA driving massive bandwidth breakthroughs and architectural shifts—including "context-aware" storage and AI-powered mobile networks—to sustain the next generation of AI scale.

Hardware Breakthroughs and the Vera Rubin Platform

  • ConnectX-9 SuperNIC: NVIDIA announced a 1.6 terabyte-per-port NIC (capable of dual 800 gig), providing the massive throughput required to feed high-performance GPUs.
  • NVLink 6 and Switch: This updated interconnect allows for 72 GPUs in a single rack to communicate at 1.8 terabytes, optimizing all-GPU communication systems.
  • Spectrum-6 Ethernet: A 102-terabyte switch that advances Ethernet to be more "InfiniBand-like."
      • Performance Convergence: While InfiniBand remains the gold standard for training, the gap with Ethernet is narrowing; for inferencing, the performance difference is now negligible.
  • Scale-Up Architecture: The Vera Rubin platform integrates CPUs, GPUs, and DPUs into a unified system designed for maximum data throughput.

Evolution of Storage and DPUs

  • BlueField-4 STX Storage: NVIDIA is repurposing the BlueField Data Processing Unit (DPU) as an offload engine for storage, enabling "context-aware storage."
      • Latency Reduction: By running storage services (like vector embeddings) directly on the DPU, organizations can perform data processing closer to the AI, reducing round-trip latency.
  • Architectural Shift: Jensen Huang noted that in AI environments, storage will increasingly connect via the East-West network rather than the traditional North-South network.
  • OEM Integration: Major vendors like HPE, Dell, and VAST Data are leaning into this DPU-centric model to bring storage services closer to the data stack.

The Rise of AI RAN and the "Distributed Neo Cloud"

  • AI at the Edge: NVIDIA’s AI RAN (Radio Access Network) vision aims to transform every mobile base station and central office into an "AI factory."
      • Latency Benefits: This allows AI workloads to operate within milliseconds of the end user, facilitating real-time analytics for smart cities and autonomous fleets.
  • The Monetization Challenge: While technically sound, speakers questioned how telecommunications companies will monetize this expensive infrastructure.
      • The "Neo Cloud" Model: Telcos may act as distributed cloud providers, utilizing idle GPU cycles at the network edge to sell tokens or AI services to customers.

Strategic Market Position: "Vertically Integrated, Horizontally Open"

  • The NVIDIA Ecosystem: The company's competitive "moat" is not just the GPU hardware, but a deeply integrated software and networking stack that remains open to partners like Cisco.
  • Cisco Partnership: A new collaboration embeds the Cisco security suite into the NVIDIA AI fabric to transition from "AI factories" to "Secure AI factories."
  • Market Advantage: Unlike the closed ecosystem of Apple or the fragmented nature of Android, NVIDIA is pursuing a model that is vertically integrated for performance but horizontally open for ecosystem stickiness.

Notable Quotes

"They realized that if they were going to accomplish what they wanted, they needed a different type of network to connect the GPUs... from very early days, they've understood the importance of networking." — Bob Laliberte
"The networking is still the hardest part of making these applications work. And the most expensive... the expertise just doesn't exist yet." — Rob Strechay
"NVIDIA's model is interesting... they are vertically integrated horizontally open. The moat around NVIDIA isn't the GPU; it's that entire ecosystem around them." — Zeus Kerravala
"In AI environments, storage will be connecting using the east-west network and not the north-south network when you're using BlueField for AI." — Bob Laliberte

Dario, Demis, Sam: Assessing the AI Guys | Robert Wright & Shakeel Hashim

Nonzero(76.5K) ·48:09 ·1.3K views · 2026-04-09
Robert Wright (Host, Nonzero) Shakeel Hashim (Editor, Transformer newsletter)
The race toward Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) is currently shaped by the conflicting personalities and ideologies of a few key CEOs whose competitive drive often overrides their stated commitments to AI safety and international governance.
TL;DR The race toward Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) is currently shaped by the conflicting personalities and ideologies of a few key CEOs whose competitive drive often overrides their stated commitments to AI safety and international governance. Reputational Concerns:...

TL;DR

The race toward Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) is currently shaped by the conflicting personalities and ideologies of a few key CEOs whose competitive drive often overrides their stated commitments to AI safety and international governance.

The Character and Conduct of Sam Altman

  • Reputational Concerns: Shakeel Hashim highlights a recent *New Yorker* profile and reporting by Karen Hao that depict Altman as a "dishonest and manipulative" figure.
      • Internal Friction: Former OpenAI Chief Scientist Ilya Sutskever reportedly compiled 200 pages of notes regarding Altman’s lack of candor, which contributed to the board's attempt to remove him.
      • Interpersonal Dynamics: Dario Amodei (Anthropic) and Mira Murati (OpenAI) reportedly left or grew wary of OpenAI due to a fundamental lack of trust in Altman’s leadership.
  • The "People-Pleaser" Defense: Robert Wright suggests Altman may not be "maliciously intentioned" but rather an extreme people-pleaser who avoids confrontation by telling different parties what they want to hear, leading to contradictory commitments.

Competitive Dynamics and the "Prisoner’s Dilemma"

  • Global Strategy: Reporting indicates that OpenAI executives, specifically Greg Brockman, discussed "auctioning off" technology to various countries—including rivals like Russia and China—to create a prisoner's dilemma where nations feel forced to fund OpenAI to avoid falling behind.
  • Political Influence: Greg Brockman has become OpenAI’s primary political operative, donating heavily to Trump-aligned super PACs and "pro-innovation" groups that lobby against stringent AI regulation.
  • Market Pressures: Both speakers agree that even "safety-focused" companies like Anthropic are surrendering to market forces.
      • Policy Shifts: Anthropic recently removed a clause from its "Responsible Scaling Policy" that previously committed the company to pause development if safety thresholds weren't met.

Divergent Ideologies: Amodei vs. Hassabis

  • Dario Amodei (Anthropic): Described as the CEO most "wrestling with the contradictions" of AI risk.
      • China Hawkishness: Unlike his peers, Amodei maintains an ideological aversion to cooperation with China, advocating for "military dominance" and strict export controls in his essay "Machines of Loving Grace."
  • Demis Hassabis (Google DeepMind): Characterized by Hashim as a "capital-S Scientist" driven by a near-religious desire to solve the "secrets of the universe" (e.g., gravity, time).
      • The "Oppenheimer" Risk: Hashim expresses concern that Hassabis’s scientific curiosity may mirror the "Jurassic Park" trope: being so preoccupied with *could* they build it that they don't stop to ask if they *should*.

The Accelerating Path to the Singularity

  • Recursive Self-Improvement: The industry is entering a phase where AI tools (like Claude Code) are significantly speeding up the ability of humans to build better AI, a precursor to fully automated AI research.
  • The "First-Mover" Trap: In both corporate and geopolitical contexts, a lead of just a few months is perceived as "everything."
      • Decisive Strategic Advantage: If one lab or nation reaches a threshold first, they could theoretically use the AI to disrupt an opponent's social fabric or eliminate nuclear deterrents before the rival can react.
  • The "Mythos" Precedent: Wright notes a recent Anthropic revelation regarding "Mythos," a model capable of finding vulnerabilities in major software like Windows. While Anthropic handled this responsibly by alerting partners, it underscores the danger of such models falling into the wrong hands without international treaties.

Notable Quotes

"A recurring impression in the article is people calling Sam Altman a sociopath... the overriding sense... is that he is dishonest, manipulative, and doesn't really seem to care a lot of the time about the consequences of his actions." — Shakeel Hashim
"[Demis Hassabis] really seems motivated by this desire to understand the secrets of the universe... he says that there are lots of things in the world that we don't understand, and what I want to do with my company is try and understand God." — Shakeel Hashim
"Dario Amodei isn't the hero we need... he has this ideological aversion almost to working things out with China... he used the phrase 'military dominance' over China." — Robert Wright
"We are seeing progress in AI accelerate the development of AI... once you hit that point of a fully automated AI R&D researcher, things start getting very crazy very quickly." — Shakeel Hashim
Hi, this is Bob. So, a quick preface to this conversation with Shakil Hashim of the Transformer newsletter. Shakil and I taped this conversation right before the AI world got some big and kind of unsettling news, which is that Anthropic revealed that a not-yet-released model called Mythos had demonstrated the ability to find vulnerabilities in all kinds of software, including widely used software like Windows. Because this news broke right before we spoke, we don't address it in the conversation, but I wanted to note how many points of contact it has with the conversation we did have. So, for starters, we agreed in the course of this conversation that AI very much needs to be governed at the international level. This is certainly a good example of that because if a bad actor in any country gets a hold of software with this power—the power that Mythos apparently has—that could be bad news for people in all kinds of countries. So it's not enough to govern these things at just the national level. We also agreed, kind of later in the conversation, that to get the world to focus on the need for international governance, you may have to have some kind of catastrophe. But we hoped that what you would have instead is just kind of a near miss, something that gets the world's attention to the potential for catastrophe. I'm hoping that news like this could serve that purpose and could get people's attention. And, you know, Claude Code was itself in a way a big accelerant, right? I mean, why don't you give me your take on kind of where we are in that regard? As I said, you're hearing more and more serious discussion within AI, like "this is—we are feeling the Singularity approaching here because the technology, especially in the Claude Code, you know, powerfully agentic era—we are seeing progress in AI accelerate the development of AI, right?" What is your sense of how real this is and how worrisome it may or may not be? Yeah, so I think it's important to, you know, distinguish between some of the hype and the reality. On the hype side, I mean, you've got Marc Andreessen said this week that AGI is here. I think that's nonsense. I don't think the models we have are AGI by any, you know, standard definition of the term. I recommend discounting anything Marc Andreessen says by about 70 percent. but go ahead. Seems right. But in terms of the point about like the approaching Singularity, I definitely think there's something there. I am hesitant to say that we're in the Singularity already. But what we definitely are in is the beginnings of what used to be called recursive self-improvement. So this idea that the AI models are improving themselves. That's overstating it, it's not quite what's happening right now. What is happening is AI models are significantly advancing the abilities of humans to improve the AI models. It's speeding this up. And you're seeing this just in like the pace of features being released in Claude Code or Codex. You're seeing it in the pace of new model releases. Those are coming thicker and faster than they ever have been. OpenAI is expected to release a new model this week. And you're seeing it in terms of the capabilities of these models is just kind of continuing to grow at an exponential rate, especially if you look at the famous "meter time rising" graph of how long these models can—what length of tasks these models can do. And this is all being driven by AI tools helping the AI engineers build these models faster and better. And when you talk to people at the labs, they all think we're really, really close to fully automating this process. So I've heard some estimates of we will have automated AI research and development engineers by the end of this year. My instinct is that is faster than it's actually going to happen. because I think there are parts of those jobs that are actually very hard to automate. maybe some of the more like taste-based aspects. Also, you've kind of just got the traditional human problems that you always run into of even if you've got unlimited resources and unlimited like intellectual horsepower, you have to coordinate people and you have to, you know, navigate the troubles of actually running an organization. But even if it's not the end of this year, the end of next year or the end of the year after that seems very plausible. And none of that is that far away. And once you hit that point of a fully automated AI R&D researcher, I think things start getting very crazy very quickly. Yeah, I think they're going to start getting a little crazy even before that when you look at how broadly this can AI can kind of affect life, you know, ranging from employment issues to safety issues to child-rearing issues and on and on. But the—this idea of more and more rapid progress, maybe toward something you could call a Singularity, is very much related to the contention that it does matter—you know, this idea that there will be this kind of one dominant superintelligence—because the idea is that once things are moving really fast, being just like six weeks ahead of another lab is everything. Because the faster things move, you know, the shorter a time span it takes to move beyond a true threshold. And like just suddenly—and this also matters in geopolitics, right? The idea is that if the US is ahead of China by only a few months, still if you reach this threshold where whichever country has the best AI, you know, the best AI is so much better than the AI of three months ago, that you can just say to the AI, "Hey, go into China's" or "go into America's social media system and like generate support for a coup and plan the coup and do the coup." Like, there are people who think you'll get to that point. and if not, that at least you'll get to the point where in sheer physical weaponry you suddenly have, you know, an order of magnitude advantage. So with both competition among labs and competition among countries, there is this idea that a small lead can suddenly give you total dominance, right? Does—does that make sense to you? That that's plausible? Yeah, I mean it's definitely plausible to me. I think my instinct is that the idea of like a truly decisive strategic advantage, I don't quite buy it. Mostly not from any particular like specific reason, but more from a general prior that the world is really, really complicated. Even a superintelligence will run into bottlenecks and problems. But I don't know, I can't foresee what they are. But sometimes you get people talking about how superintelligence will eliminate the nuclear deterrent because it will invent some clever technology that lets you spot the nuclear submarine through the oceans using satellite imagery. Then America will know where all of China's nuclear submarines are and then you can eliminate them and then the idea of nuclear deterrence is over. Stories like that just always sound a little bit too neat to me. Just in the real world, things are complicated, things take time. I don't know if I'd want to, you know, bet on that as our our plan for safety, of it's all going to be fine because something will happen and it'll be okay. but it does make me like a little bit skeptical of some of those ideas that you get this decisive advantage that no one else can then catch up to you. So maybe I should let you give us your kind of thumbnail take on some of the other players. We've talked a little about Sam and is there one that you would most trust if you imagine that, yeah, it's really going to matter at some point that one of them be a person of character and virtue and really have the planet's interest at heart? So this idea that you get the nations to—to say, "Hey, if you don't invest in OpenAI, all these other nations are, and then you're not going to have the best technology." Not a crazy business model, actually, but and I think I think they would say, just—just in fairness—and I think Brockman is quoted as—as saying this, or somebody is, "Hey, we were just batting around ideas." and it's hard to tell from the reporting, you know. I've been in journalism enough to know that one should be a little skeptical about recreations of scenes and context and so on. Anyway, I want to put that aside for now and ask you, you know, to size up—you must have views of these people by now—kind of relative to one another, like—like what—what you like about them, what you don't. what would you say? Yeah, it's tricky in that, you know, so much of what we know is just—I mean hearsay, for lack of a better word. I mean good deep reporting, but it's—often you're only getting one side of the story. BREAKING: JD Vance Flags Billions in Fraud Probe

OfficialACLJ(567.0K) ·59:16 ·16.9K views · 2026-04-09
JD Vance (Vice President of the United States)
Vice President JD Vance’s newly established task force has identified approximately $6.3 billion in potentially fraudulent government contracts, while Vance simultaneously prepares to lead a high-stakes diplomatic delegation to Pakistan for peace talks with Iran.
TL;DR Vice President JD Vance’s newly established task force has identified approximately $6.3 billion in potentially fraudulent government contracts, while Vance simultaneously prepares to lead a high-stakes diplomatic delegation to Pakistan for peace talks with Iran. Rapid...

TL;DR

Vice President JD Vance’s newly established task force has identified approximately $6.3 billion in potentially fraudulent government contracts, while Vance simultaneously prepares to lead a high-stakes diplomatic delegation to Pakistan for peace talks with Iran.

Federal Fraud Task Force Results

  • Rapid Identification: In less than a month since its inception via executive order, the task force chaired by Vice President JD Vance has flagged nearly $6.3 billion in suspicious government contracts.
  • Targeted Businesses: Letters have been sent to approximately 400 businesses currently holding government contracts that exhibit "suspicious" characteristics.
      • 30-Day Deadline: These entities have 30 days to provide proof of physical existence and legitimate business operations or risk immediate contract cancellation.
  • Scope of Investigation: The probe is targeting massive federal programs, including:
      • Medicare & Medicaid: Programs spending roughly $2 trillion annually.
      • Unemployment Insurance: Identifying waste within the Department of Labor’s $43 billion annual spending.
      • Criminal Referrals: The task force is making recommendations to the Department of Justice, which currently has over 8,000 active fraud matters underway.

State-Level Systemic Fraud (California)

  • Manhattan Institute Report: An exposé by Chris Rufo (City Journal) alleges that the state of California has lost at least $180 billion to fraud under Governor Gavin Newsom’s administration.
      • Economic Comparison: The amount lost is equivalent to the entire GDP of New Zealand.
  • Primary Fraud Sources: The report identifies three main areas of systemic abuse:
      • Medi-Cal: The state’s Medicaid program.
      • Unemployment Insurance: Fraudulent claims during and after the pandemic.
      • General Welfare: Abuse of self-regulated assistance programs.
  • Accountability Gap: Mike Pompeo and Rick Grenell noted that while the state pursues relatively small fines against religious organizations (e.g., a $1.2 million COVID-19 fine against Calvary Chapel), massive systemic fraud has gone largely unpunished due to a lack of oversight in one-party governance.

Diplomatic Mission to Iran

  • Islamabad Peace Talks: Vice President Vance will lead the U.S. delegation to Islamabad, Pakistan, for high-level negotiations with Iranian officials.
      • Delegation Members: The team includes Jared Kushner and Steve Witkoff.
  • Strategic Redlines: Former Secretary of State Mike Pompeo emphasized that the U.S. remains focused on "verifying rather than trusting." Key objectives include:
      • Nuclear Enrichment: Total cessation of Iranian uranium enrichment.
      • Maritime Security: Reopening the Strait of Hormuz to free transit without Iranian "tolls."
      • Proxy Activity: Ending the funding of terror groups and proxies like Hezbollah.
  • Leadership Legitimacy: A primary goal of the meeting is to determine if the current Iranian representatives have the actual authority to deliver on promises following recent regional hostilities.

Legal and Judicial Concerns

  • Leniency in Sentencing: The briefing highlighted a recent Minnesota case where individuals who admitted to stealing $5.6 million from the "Feed Our Future" program received sentences as short as six months to one year.
  • Judicial Double Standards: Guest speakers criticized the legal system for "venue shopping" to block federal efficiency efforts (DOGE) while failing to provide a deterrent for high-level financial crimes against taxpayers.

Ex-Tesla President: Elon Behind-The-Scenes, Saving Tesla & Scaling a Trillion-Dollar Company

My First Million(892.0K) ·62:45 ·6.7K views · 2026-04-09
Jon McNeill (Former President, Tesla) Shaan Puri (Host, My First Million)
1.
TL;DR: Former Tesla President Jon McNeill reveals "The Algorithm"—a management framework centered on setting 10X "order of magnitude" goals, identifying market friction through frontline observation, and radical simplification of complex business models. The "Deep Dive"...

TL;DR: Former Tesla President Jon McNeill reveals "The Algorithm"—a management framework centered on setting 10X "order of magnitude" goals, identifying market friction through frontline observation, and radical simplification of complex business models.

The Elon Musk Hiring Method: Testing for World-Class Talent

  • The "Deep Dive" Interrogation: Musk’s interview process bypasses small talk to focus on a specific, complex problem the candidate has solved. He drills down through layers of detail to determine if the candidate actually did the work or was simply a "passenger" on a successful team.
  • The "Wow" Metric: The goal is to be impressed ("Wow, wow, wow") within the first 20 minutes. If that level of insight isn't reached quickly, the candidate is likely not a fit for high-intensity roles.
  • Talent as the Primary Driver: At Tesla’s peak growth, McNeill spent 60% of his calendar on interviews. Musk and co-founder JB Straubel insisted on being the final interviewers for every manager-level hire to "imprint the culture" on the organization.
  • Hiring "Fishermen" vs. "Order Takers":
      • The Apple vs. Microsoft Store Heuristic: McNeill warns against hiring from "perfect" environments (like Apple stores) where the product sells itself. He prefers hiring from competitors (like Microsoft stores) where salespeople must fight for every sale against a dominant market leader.

First Principles Problem Solving: "Eyes and Ears" Analytics

  • Ground Floor Truth: McNeill advocates for "mystery shopping" and sitting in warehouses/factories for hours without an agenda.
      • The 9,000 Lead Discovery: Before joining Tesla, McNeill mystery-shopped eight stores and received zero follow-up calls. He discovered 9,000 "dead" leads in the CRM and solved the quarterly sales goal simply by forcing staff to call them back.
  • The "Two Eyes, Two Ears" Rule: Relying solely on data reports is a trap. McNeill suggests physical observation to identify process constraints.
      • The Model X Jig: While struggling with Falcon Wing door alignment, McNeill and Musk watched workers on the line for an hour. They realized workers were "threading bolts blind." The solution was a simple physical jig, not a complex software fix.
  • The "Follow Me Home" Strategy: Borrowed from Intuit’s Scott Cook, this involves watching customers use a product in their natural environment to see where they struggle or what they wish the product could do (e.g., how the Swiffer was invented by watching moms struggle with mops).

Driving 10X Growth Through Radical Simplification

  • Order of Magnitude Goals: Musk sets 10X or 20X goals (e.g., 20X digital sales) because they force employees to abandon "tweaking" and rethink the entire system from scratch.
  • Eliminating Decision Fatigue: To hit aggressive online sales targets, McNeill reduced the Tesla purchase process from 64 clicks to 10 (matching the Domino’s Pizza app).
      • Configuration Collapse: He challenged the "religion" of custom-built cars, discovering that despite 360,000 possible combinations, customers only really cared about two: "Performance" or "Long Range." Reducing choices simplified the factory and engineering pipelines simultaneously.
  • Stack-Ranking Constraints: A leader's primary job is to stack-rank the problems constraining the business and "pull the biggest problem off the top of the pile every day."

Identifying Market Opportunities in "Friction"

  • The "One Size Fits All" Trap: McNeill looks for industries where all competitors offer the same rigid solution.
      • Cyber Insurance Example: He noticed a retailer and a data-heavy tech firm had identical $7M insurance premiums despite vastly different risks. This signaled a market failure, leading to a successful startup opportunity.
  • Cycle Time vs. Touch Time: In the collision repair industry, McNeill found an 18-day "cycle time" for only 6-8 hours of "touch time" (actual labor).
      • Industrialization of Service: By shifting from a "hair salon" model (one tech, one car) to a Henry Ford-style assembly line, his company (Sterling/Service King) returned cars in days rather than weeks, disrupting a $50B industry.

Executive Communication: The Three-Sentence Rule

  • Maximizing "Compute" Time: To communicate with high-bandwidth CEOs like Musk, McNeill uses a hyper-efficient structure to minimize information absorption time.
  • The Structure:

1. What: Identify the problem.

2. Why: Identify the root cause.

3. So What: Propose a specific solution with cost/benefit analysis.

  • Benefit: This doesn't just save the CEO time; it forces the sender to achieve absolute clarity in their own thinking.

***

The 50-State Plan: Public-Private Models for AI Infrastructure and University Transformation

NVIDIA(2.2M) ·53:02 ·1.3K views · 2026-04-09
The "50-State Plan" is a strategic initiative to democratize AI by establishing high-performance computing infrastructure in every U.S.
TL;DR The "50-State Plan" is a strategic initiative to democratize AI by establishing high-performance computing infrastructure in every U.S. state through public-private partnerships, ensuring that AI literacy and research capabilities drive regional economic growth across all...

TL;DR

The "50-State Plan" is a strategic initiative to democratize AI by establishing high-performance computing infrastructure in every U.S. state through public-private partnerships, ensuring that AI literacy and research capabilities drive regional economic growth across all academic disciplines.

The "50-State Solution" and Public-Private Partnerships

  • The Florida Model as a Blueprint: Chris Malachowsky (NVIDIA Co-founder) detailed the success of gifting a supercomputer to the University of Florida, which served as a catalyst for statewide AI literacy and a $200 million state investment in faculty.
  • NVIDIA’s Commitment: To scale this nationwide, NVIDIA announced it will:
      • Match Contributions: Provide discounts to match donor contributions for university AI infrastructure.
      • Software Access: Provide its world-class software stack to universities for free.
      • Priority Access: Prioritize the delivery of advanced technology to support U.S. economic infrastructure.
  • Capitalizing on Philanthropy: The model relies on "paying it forward," encouraging private benefactors in every state to fund hardware that serves as a regional economic engine rather than just an engineering tool.

National Infrastructure and Regional Innovation

  • The National AI Research Resource (NAIRR): Brian Stone (NSF) highlighted the NAIRR pilot, a federal-private partnership (including NVIDIA) that currently supports over 600 projects.
      • Scope of Research: Interestingly, 60% of NAIRR projects are in non-AI fields like material science and geophysics, demonstrating that AI is now the primary tool for all scientific discovery.
  • Regional Innovation Engines: The NSF’s "TIP" (Technology, Innovation, and Partnerships) directorate is focused on creating regional hubs that align with local economic needs, such as agriculture in the Midwest or power systems in the Carolinas.
  • Consortium Models: States are moving away from "bespoke servers under desks" toward unified strategies:
      • AI Tennessee: A statewide consortium including technical colleges, community colleges, and four-year institutions to share expertise.
      • Keystone AI (Pennsylvania): A historic MOU signed by all seven R1 research institutions in the state to share resources and infrastructure.

Transforming the Workforce and Curriculum

  • Universal AI Literacy: Panelists emphasized that AI must be "the subject of learning and integrated into learning" across every department.
      • The Humanities Gap: Deborah Crawford (U of Tennessee) noted that the highest engagement for AI fellowships often comes from non-STEM faculty (Arts, Law, Social Sciences) seeking to understand how the technology reshapes their fields.
  • Community College Integration: A major priority is ensuring AI access isn't limited to elite "R1" universities.
      • Democratization: Success is defined by providing community colleges and minority-serving institutions with the same compute cycles as top-tier research centers to prevent a new digital divide.
  • The "Eds and Meds" Strategy: Theresa Mayer (Carnegie Mellon) cited Pittsburgh’s transition from steel to a high-tech economy as a model for how university-led AI research can rebuild a region’s manufacturing base.

Success Metrics for the Next Decade

  • Computation as a Utility: Chris Malachowsky argued that computational resources must eventually be viewed as public infrastructure, similar to water or electricity.
  • Physical AI and Robotics: The next frontier is "Sim-to-Real"—using AI to control physical systems, autonomous labs, and advanced manufacturing.
  • Economic Outcomes: Success will be measured by:
      • Placement Statistics: Doubling or tripling student job placement rates.
      • Research Funding: Increasing the flow of federal and private research dollars into state economies.
      • Startup Incubation: Providing "near-term" compute access to university startups so they remain in-state rather than migrating to tech hubs.
"The machine is really just a catalyst. It's a piece of iron. It's what you do with it that's kind of magical... The 50-state solution [ensures] every state has someone willing to promote their institutions on the same technological footing as the best in the world." — Chris Malachowsky, NVIDIA Co-founder
"Within a few short years—or now—everybody is going to be an AI researcher in some way, because we will all use it. We have to embrace it and lean into it." — Brian Stone, Chief of Staff, National Science Foundation
"Success is us shifting our thinking. AI is not 'this or that'—AI is a cross-cut. It is the engine for the future of our nation." — Theresa Mayer, VP for Research, Carnegie Mellon University
Welcome to GTC. So good to see you here. You know, AI is reshaping not only research and engineering and enterprise; AI is having a huge impact on higher education. And that's what we're here to talk about today: the role of AI in higher education infrastructures for education and also for research and other mission-related activities that the universities have to pursue. We're very fortunate to have a stellar panel and I want to bring the panel out and then I'll introduce them. Please come out, panel. So first, I'm very happy to introduce Chris Malachowsky, NVIDIA Co-founder and technology leader from the very beginning of NVIDIA. Chris has a passion for higher education and AI and what high-performance computing can bring. Also, we have available Brian Stone, who is the chief of staff of the National Science Foundation, acting in the role of the director. We have Deborah Crawford, who is the Vice Chancellor for Research at the University of Tennessee and a leader in the regional activities there. And Theresa Mayer, who is the Vice President for Research at Carnegie Mellon, who is very much a leader in activities at Carnegie Mellon. So, there's a tremendous urgency around this topic today. What we're talking about has critical importance. There is a need to get a curriculum in place. Also important that we're able to get infrastructure on the campuses so that educators and researchers can participate fully in the innovation ecosystem that's going on in the world today and continue to drive things forward in a very, very important area. So much innovation comes from higher education and research, and of course, higher education research is adjacent to all of our enterprises and industry and so it's just critically important that they're able to fully participate. So our goal today is to have a candid, forward-looking conversation. What does this new era of AI and accelerated computing demand from us as researchers, institutions, and policymakers? How do we get the necessary infrastructure into the hands of students and professors at our universities? And what new partnerships are emerging, and what still needs to be done, that still needs to be invented that we haven't created yet to fill the gaps? So with that, let's get started. So, my first question is for Chris. Chris, you've seen NVIDIA and accelerated computing from the very beginning. When you look at today's AI and computing landscape, what feels fundamentally different than say in the past? What feels fundamentally different about this moment, and what does it mean for universities and national research agencies over the next five or ten years? Okay, well let's take those in order. As a technological revolution, this is broader, it's deeper, it's more impactful to more people than anything, you know, I've been in this 40-something years. So just in scale is one element. The other one is in this speed of adoption or disruption. It has infiltrated so much so quickly, taken over the public dialogue of technology and jobs and workforce. And that's unique. Most other things, at least PC and iPhones and apps and social media, came on slowly and affected a segment and then grew. This just seems to be a bit splat. It's everywhere. So I think that to the first point. What's different is also that the perception of what AI is I think is a little bit distorted in the public's mind. It's a chatbot. It's a place you type. And I think that really under-represents the potential of AI which is going to be the decision support system for just about everything we do and should be because it brings expertise where we may not have it and can gather intelligence that's hard to keep in one person's head. So I think it's a challenge for the educators. I think tasks are likely to get automated but not judgment and not the human element. And now you've got to teach everything you used to have to teach and now you've got to teach judgment and how to manage agents and how to use these new tools to your advantage and not be used by them, I guess. Maybe one of our university leaders would like to contribute to that question. Yeah, I'd be happy to jump in. And just to add to what Chris shared, I think so many people think that AI is about large language models and it's so much more than that. Accelerated compute has provided the scale that now allows us to train foundation models for science, foundation models for robotics, and just how quickly we're seeing the ability to now leverage AI in the context of physical AI where we have systems that are interacting with the real world in very complex environments. And the power now of being able to bring physics-based models to problems where data was very sparse. We don't have the training data and this concept of sim-to-real just taking off like crazy. So it's been exciting. Carnegie Mellon's had three startup companies just within the last couple of years, Skild AI, Genesis AI, and Field AI, that are all really kind of targeting this, you know, generalized brain for physical AI. So there's so many different areas where we need the scale. I'd add a little bit to that, Jack. You know, I think the pace of discovery and innovation definitely really accelerating now. I think we're also thinking about how AI has to be incorporated both into learning and be the subject of learning across all domains in the university environment. I know at UT, we established our first faculty governance committee around AI in 2022 really to get faculty not just from our engineering and computer science programs but from programs throughout the university thinking about how AI was relevant to their students, to their programs. We've created a GenAI faculty fellow program that actually encourages faculty to incorporate AI into their curriculum, encourages them to team teach. We put together teams of these sort of trailblazer faculty who are very excited about what AI is going to do for their discipline, as well as perhaps some healthy skeptics who might be worried about doing this team teaching, exploring the sort of impact on learning in our student population, and making sure that every one of our students when they graduate has AI competencies, understands why AI is relevant to their field of study, and is ready to go out into the workforce prepared to use AI. We hear that from employers who hire our students very, very often. The employers we work with tend to be more interested in sort of the workforce implications than discovery to begin with. So that's been really, really critical to us is sort of making sure that we are integrating AI throughout the academic enterprise. Thank you. Brian, I'd like to come to you next. Great. Well, first of all, thanks for having us here. Let me just say because I'll say it upfront, NSF is very grateful for the partnerships that we have with NVIDIA.
