We've got to this point by years and years, decades of thoughtful investment and moving the ball forward. And it's great when you see these technologies get to the point where people are saying, wow, it's in my phone or I'm using it every day. That's success for us. I'm going to add to what Chris said. I think this inflection point is because we've reached that point where we're all using AI on a daily basis. And I think that when we talk about the kind of bets going forward in the future, what both Theresa and Deborah are saying is 100% correct. We have to do everything that we can to integrate AI into the disciplines that we support. We need to make sure that people have the skillsets and the competencies to do it. NSF has been making some pretty major investments over the last six or seven years. In 2019, for example, we launched our AI Institutes program and we have 29 of them today. We have 29 institutes, we've got over $300 million in private contributions to that, which is one of the other things that we like to see. NSF likes to put in money and we like to see that matched by contributions either from private or philanthropy to make it a bigger enterprise. In the case of the AI Institutes, it's really hitting a sweet spot for us in this concept of regional innovation. So we have our new Technology Innovation and Partnerships Directorate. I say it's new but it's like four years old now, so that's still new for us. But TIP and also the rest of NSF is very much unlocking these regional area focuses. So for example, if you're someone that lives in Iowa, you may care about agriculture a lot more than you care about other things. And I think what we're finding is universities in these areas, which already serve as kind of the anchor points and the powerhouses to drive the economy, they're able to tap into AI and make it relevant for the people in the areas that they serve. And I think that's one of the things that's the most exciting for us. The other piece that's very critical, I mentioned the workforce piece. This, all of the things that we're doing, it's activating education at all levels. It's at the four-year university levels. We've had tremendous interest from community colleges. I mean, we were just down in Charlotte a couple of weeks ago at one of our regional innovation engine site visits. We had all of the community colleges from South Carolina and North Carolina there talking about what NSF is going to do to make their lives better and help them do better. It was focused on power, but how they're going to create more jobs. We don't typically focus on, we don't wake up and say how many jobs are we going to create today at NSF, but that's the kind of impact that we're starting to have and that's what's really exciting for us. So investment in regional technology hubs, investment in personnel, we think are the most critical. I'll just comment there from partnerships from industry having a focus on regional economic development provides a focus point for us to engage because it brings a set of priorities to a university that otherwise is doing in some sense everything in science and education, technology, and we very much appreciate the opportunity to focus on what your priorities are because then we can focus that interaction and hopefully demonstrate in some reasonable time period a return on our mutual investment. Super. So back to Deborah and Theresa, what's your comment about this idea of regional focus for AI investment and a way of moving that forward in our universities and then the ecosystem around them? So I think NSF has done a really fabulous job in helping universities think more strategically about regional partnerships. I think TIP has really had huge impact in that realm here in the United States. In Tennessee, we formed a consortium, it's called AI Tennessee, and to Brian's point, it's a consortium that includes all of the colleges and universities in the state of Tennessee, our technical colleges, our community colleges, and our four-year institutions, working together to figure out how we make sure that every Tennessean has the ability to develop AI competencies and is confident in their use. It also has a research and innovation component and it's very much built on a backbone of public-private partnerships. Yeah, well, I'm just going to start by telling a little story. Pittsburgh, with the steel industry, Pittsburgh built the nation. And then Pittsburgh collapsed in the 80s but left behind a very strong manufacturing base. But at that point, the collapse was so severe that the nation, the region, the state, and the local government came together and said we're going to take an eds and meds strategy given the presence of University of Pittsburgh, Carnegie Mellon, many other universities, a very robust community college. And that drove a renaissance, that vision to pull together. And it's one of the few cities outside of the coasts that now has a growth starting about a decade ago in the innovation economy. So it is truly an example of what so many of the initiatives that are really at the forefront right now kind of got off to an early start: robotics, AI. Now we have this very, very interesting mix of the fusion of a deep manufacturing base together with the high-tech industries of the future. And this is late 2010s, 2018, 2019. Ultimately, we gifted a supercomputer, big AI supercomputer, to the University of Florida and a little fortuitously, I didn't give it to the engineering department, I gave it to the university. Which had the nice ramification of the university deciding, this is 2019, early 2020, this is COVID, you know, AI was not that big a deal yet, at least the impact wasn't being felt, I'd been lecturing them to get in front of it before it got on them. And because I gave the gift to the university, they turned around and committed, just like we're just hearing, to teach AI literacy in every department. Big R1, comprehensive university, they're outputting something like 10,000 AI-literate kids in every discipline since then. They were a land grant, like we heard, land grants have this obligation and incentive to be part of the economics of their state. They offered it for use by every researcher in the state, same terms as if you're an on-campus researcher. They offered it free for educational use, you're a high schooler and you want to run on this supercomputer, great, your professor gives you assignments. They developed all this curriculum. The state, at 2020 you can imagine there wasn't a whole lot of AI faculty or AI-literate faculty. The state ended up putting in a couple hundred million dollars over 10 years to fund 100 faculty across the university complex. And then you get to the end, they extended the use of the machine to the entire SEC, by the way, and all the historically Black schools and minority schools. And then, you know, turns out they're willing to give all that away to anybody who wants it. And you know, I feel fortunate that I was observant enough to recognize this as what was potentially a model because I was worried, just like we're hearing, that if we don't get in front of AI, yes you could displace jobs. Yes it could, the rhetoric would get ahead of the reality and it would stymie progress. So I've taken it upon myself, using my platform as NVIDIA co-founder where I can get myself into places maybe others can't, and I've been offering to... It's been very well received and it's addressing all the things we just heard. It's good for research, but it's good for the communities, it's good for the students, it's good for employment. I think UF doubled or tripled their placement statistics, doubled or tripled their research dollars coming in, impacting the state economy. And I figure every state can do this. Every state has somebody willing to give some number of dollars to promote the football team or endow a building or a program. 'SMOKE AND MIRRORS': Iran FIRES off missiles, call Trump peace talks 'MEANINGLESS'

Fox Business(3.3M) ·21:30 ·271.7K views · 2026-04-09

White House National Economic Council Director Kevin Hassett predicts a "Golden Age" of 4–5% economic growth driven by AI productivity, despite temporary market volatility from the Iran conflict.
TL;DR White House National Economic Council Director Kevin Hassett predicts a "Golden Age" of 4–5% economic growth driven by AI productivity, despite temporary market volatility from the Iran conflict. The administration remains confident in weekend peace talks in Islamabad...

TL;DR

White House National Economic Council Director Kevin Hassett predicts a "Golden Age" of 4–5% economic growth driven by AI productivity, despite temporary market volatility from the Iran conflict. The administration remains confident in weekend peace talks in Islamabad while threatening 50% tariffs on any nation supplying weapons to Tehran.

Geopolitical Tensions and Iran Negotiations

  • Islamabad Peace Talks: A high-level U.S. delegation, including Jared Kushner and Steve Witkoff, is heading to Pakistan for weekend negotiations.
      • Hassett dismisses claims that talks are "meaningless," stating that the Iranian military and leadership have been largely "eliminated," leaving them with little authority but a strong desire to rejoin the global banking and trade systems.
  • Strait of Hormuz: Iran has reportedly attempted to tighten control over the Strait, allegedly charging tankers millions in fees. Hassett expects this to be temporary, noting that "rogue folks" may still be firing missiles because they haven't received word of a ceasefire.
  • Pressure Tactics: President Trump has threatened 50% tariffs on any country—specifically eyeing those supporting Iran's military—utilizing IEEPA (International Emergency Economic Powers Act) authorities.

Economic Outlook and the "AI Golden Age"

  • Aggressive Growth Forecast: Despite a difficult quarter for stocks, Hassett maintains a GDP growth forecast of 4–5% for the year.
      • Productivity Drivers: AI is already contributing roughly 2.5% to productivity. Small businesses adopting AI have reportedly seen revenues grow by 100% over the last year.
  • Investment Boom: Orders and shipments for capital goods are currently at all-time highs, which Hassett attributes to optimism surrounding deregulation and tax policies.
  • Market Volatility: The administration views current oil price spikes and market dips as "temporary distractions" that will resolve once negotiations conclude.

Energy Independence and Global Supply

  • Oil Price Disruption: While oil prices rose roughly 4.5% to $98/barrel, Hassett notes that futures markets expect prices to drop by $20–$30 within a month as the Straits reopen.
  • Infrastructure Status: Analysis suggests that despite the conflict, critical energy infrastructure remains operational or quickly repairable.
  • U.S. Export Opportunities: Allies, particularly in Asia, are facing severe shortages. The U.S. is seeing increased interest from allies in "off-take agreements" for guaranteed supplies of American oil and gas to ensure long-term energy security.

Federal Reserve and Monetary Policy

  • Leadership Transition: Hassett is "highly confident" that Kevin Warsh will be confirmed and begin as Fed Chairman on May 15.
      • Jay Powell’s Exit: The White House expects current Chair Jay Powell to step aside entirely once a successor is confirmed, rather than remaining on the Board of Governors.
  • Rate Strategy: Hassett argues that the Fed should "look through" the current oil supply shock, as economic literature suggests temporary supply disruptions should not dictate long-term interest rate hikes.
  • Balance Sheet Reduction: The administration supports a gradual, prudent reduction of the Fed's balance sheet to return to "normal" pre-2008 levels.

Fiscal Policy and Government Spending

  • Spending Cuts: Hassett highlighted the elimination of over 300,000 federal jobs, which is projected to save nearly $1 trillion over the next decade.
  • Legislative Priorities: The administration is pushing for a reconciliation bill by June 1, with a primary focus on funding the Department of Homeland Security (DHS) and increasing defense spending to $1.5 trillion by 2027.
  • Deficit Reduction: Higher-than-expected growth and tariff revenues reportedly reduced the deficit by $600 billion last year.

Investigating Altman With Ronan Farrow and Andrew Marantz | Better Offline

Better Offline(22.5K) ·43:02 ·6.1K views · 2026-04-09
An investigation into OpenAI CEO Sam Altman reveals a documented pattern of perceived manipulation and "circular" financial dealings that prioritize investor interests over the company’s original safety-focused non-profit mission.
TL;DR An investigation into OpenAI CEO Sam Altman reveals a documented pattern of perceived manipulation and "circular" financial dealings that prioritize investor interests over the company’s original safety-focused non-profit mission. While Altman positions himself as a...

TL;DR

An investigation into OpenAI CEO Sam Altman reveals a documented pattern of perceived manipulation and "circular" financial dealings that prioritize investor interests over the company’s original safety-focused non-profit mission. While Altman positions himself as a messianic figure essential to humanity’s future, critics and former colleagues describe a leader whose habitual dissembling has created a fundamental crisis of trust at the heart of the AI industry.

The "Blip": Unpacking Altman’s Firing and Return

  • Cumulative Manipulation: Andrew Marantz notes that Altman’s firing by the board was not caused by a single "smoking gun" event but by a "subtle, slower accumulation" of manipulations.
      • Altman reportedly told different stories to different stakeholders, promising safety to non-profit researchers while promising massive returns to venture capitalists.
  • The Victory of Capitalism: Ed Zitron and Ronan Farrow characterize Altman’s return as a moment where "capitalism won."
      • Despite the board’s mandate to prioritize humanity’s safety, the pressure from Microsoft (Satya Nadella) and investors (Brian Chesky) to protect their capital outweighed the board’s concerns regarding Altman’s integrity.
  • Lack of Transparency: The law firm investigation into the firing resulted in no written report other than an 800-word press release.
      • Farrow highlights that this lack of documentation is highly irregular for a high-profile scandal intended to "restore trust" and could pose legal risks during a future IPO.

Financial Risks and Circular Dealings

  • High Cash Burn: OpenAI has one of the fastest cash burn rates in startup history, requiring what Farrow describes as "almost all the money in the world" to reach Artificial General Intelligence (AGI).
  • Circular Ecosystems: Critics allege the company is involved in "circular deals" where partners borrow money to purchase each other’s products, artificially inflating valuations.
      • Microsoft Tensions: Recent deals, such as the Amazon partnership for enterprise products, have reportedly created friction with Microsoft regarding the exclusivity of underlying models.
  • Middle Eastern Funding: To sustain growth, Altman has aggressively courted Middle Eastern capital (UAE/Saudi), even bypassing standard security clearance vetting processes that raised "red flags" within the national security establishment.

The Safety Paradox and Messianic Ambition

  • Rhetorical Slippage: Marantz explains how Altman redefined "safety" and "alignment" as the company evolved.
      • In 2015, "alignment" meant preventing human extinction; by 2023, Altman compared it to the "annoyance" of an Instagram algorithm.
  • The Messiah Complex: Farrow describes Altman as having a "messianic quality," viewing himself as a singular figure whose personal power is synonymous with humanity's success.
      • Altman reportedly told recruits he intended for OpenAI to "capture the light cone of all the value in the universe."
  • Psychological Profile: Multiple sources unprompted used the word "sociopath" to describe Altman. Farrow noted a lack of deep self-reflection when Altman was confronted with allegations of compulsive lying, with Altman offering only "anodyne" responses about therapy and breathwork.

Geopolitical Maneuvering

  • Sovereign Bidding Wars: The transcript discusses early internal ideas—credited to co-founder Greg Brockman—about auctioning powerful AI technology to world powers like the U.S., China, and Russia to spark a bidding war.
      • While this did not manifest, the fact that such "volatile" game theory was discussed and pitched to investors indicates the scale of the company’s ambition.
  • Supergovernmental Status: Farrow argues that Silicon Valley figures like Altman and Elon Musk now view themselves as "supergovernmental," holding levers of power that traditionally belonged to the state.

Notable Quotes

"He has set up the rationales in his mind... so that him winning and consolidating as much power as possible is the same thing as humanity winning." — Ronan Farrow
"Sam Altman did not go to everyone and say, 'AI's gonna be a really big deal... Let's all go and make a bunch of money.' What he was saying was, 'You engineers, you need to come along with me... because I will be circumspect, I will be safe.'" — Andrew Marantz
"There's a small but real chance Altman is eventually remembered as a Bernie Madoff or Sam Bankman-Fried level scammer." — Unnamed Microsoft Executive (quoted by Ed Zitron)
"The reality around all of these guys is we have let Silicon Valley grab all of the levers of power in the United States... it is very hard I think for the people at the forefront of that to consider themselves anything but messianic." — Ronan Farrow
Hello and welcome to Better Offline. I'm of course your host, Ed Zitron. No monologue this week, quit complaining, because I've brought you all something far better. I am joined by Ronan Farrow and Andrew Marantz of the New Yorker, who just put out a massive piece about Sam Altman, "the dampest man in Silicon Valley history"—which is my comment, not theirs. I want to start—thank you for joining me both of you, first of all. I want to ask a really just blunt question, Ronan. Considering your history of the people you've reported on, why Sam Altman? What drew you to him? Well, this is fundamentally different than stories I've done that started with, you know, a single clear-cut lead about criminality. This was a situation where I was coming off of reporting you may be familiar with about Elon Musk and his whole empire, and I had a lot of sources in the world of AI, including Sam Altman, who was on the record in that piece. And you know, this was no novel genius projection on my part. Obviously it was at a moment when AI was becoming the kind of fulcrum of Silicon Valley, and it became apparent to me that there were under-examined questions about the trajectory of the technology and the integrity of the stewards of it. We really started looking at that with an open mind. You know, Andrew is on this beat of Big Tech all the time and is also well-sourced up in this world, and we both, I think, sat down and were having our conversations about what the most salient points of contention were in this world. And we're equally open to criticism about all of the labs, all of the figures. This question of why Sam Altman was fired, what the specific alleged proof points were, and whether those questions were something we should care about—were they right to care then, and should we still care now? Are those problems ongoing? Those really emerged as just a lingering, unanswered set of questions that hadn't been fully proved out and backed up in this kind of a forensic way. Right. So why was he fired? Because the piece kind of got into it, but the answers are all over the place from everyone, and each person seems to have a different interpretation. Andrew, you want to start? Yeah, I think one of the abiding frustrations and curiosities from this is the whole world saw Sam Altman get fired, and then the whole world watched as he returned, and that was never really adequately explained in the public narrative. And so we really go into with a sort of more documentation, I think, than ever before exactly what was going on. And as Ronan says, you know, it was not this sort of one smoking gun thing. Like it wasn't, oh, you know, Sam Altman was strangling baby puppies in the office and nobody knew it until now, right? This is a much subtler, slower accumulation of what his critics say are manipulations. Telling two different stories to people. The idea of telling different people what they want to hear comes up a lot. And so this is the kind of thing where you kind of need a New Yorker story—I say sort of self-interestedly, but like, I do think that you kind of need to put all the facts in one place for them to make sense. And there have been really good books about this; there have been a lot out there. I mean, we're obviously not first to this story, but I just think it's hard to understand what the allegations even are without lining it all up in one place. And you kind of have to centrally start from this premise that Sam Altman did not go to everyone and say, "AI's gonna be a really big deal, it's gonna be a product we can try to sell, it's gonna be a good investment pitch for us. Let's all go and make a bunch of money," right? What he was saying was, "You guys, you engineers, you need to come along with me and build this technology with me specifically because I will be circumspect, I will be safe, I will argue for regulation, I will keep it in this non-profit AI safety research lab format." And it was the betrayal of those promises that they felt was the fireable offense, more so than any one smoking gun thing. Except he was kind of brought back by capitalism. I remember when it happened, there were journalists posting that they were crying with happiness about it. It was genuinely one of the darker moments I can think of in Silicon Valley history, because it's like, wow, Brian Chesky and Satya Nadella got together and held hands and got that Sam Altman back up. It was just so bizarre. It is weird, and it speaks to, you know, this thing again that people consistently allege in the documents we saw and during our interviews of this sort of telling different stories to different communities of people. I mean, one of the most basic examples of this, right, is turning to the safety non-profit people and saying, "This will always be a safety non-profit," and then turning to the VC investment community and saying, "Let's go guys, rocket to the moon, let's go make a bunch of money." And kind of simultaneously holding both of those communities seemingly in the palm of his hand until he wasn't able to anymore. You're exactly right to frame it as capitalism winning. And I think we were fascinated in this case of what's called "the blip," you know, this brief firing and then the undoing the firing, because it does feel like it is about much more than Sam Altman. The thing that OpenAI originally was, when it was founded as a non-profit and in the way Andrew described, was pitched on the basis of these fears from the people developing the technology, where the founders of this company were saying, "Listen, this is so dangerous that we're comparing it to nuclear weapons, and we need to create a noble-minded research lab without a profit motive." They recruited on that basis. People took pay cuts to go there on that basis. And this moment when the board that was empowered to remove a CEO if they felt that CEO could not be trusted with that mission—when they tried to do that, and basically investors who really didn't know what the hell was going on at the time—that's one of the things we document. You know, the board really fumbled the ball, we can talk about how—but the long and short of it is that those investors rallied around Sam Altman, who really stood to make them a lot of money, and they were worried that their money would go away if the company fell apart. And so it is a part of a bigger story about the way in which Silicon Valley builds companies based on hype, and at least not solely real value, and where the initial promises very often don't pan out. What winds up governing is—I think it's valid when people in this piece have the criticism of, you know, we're now seeing a race to the bottom on the very safety issues that OpenAI was supposed to be founded around. And the blip was a turning point that encapsulated that. Do you buy that Sam Altman gives a shit about safety? Do you actually think that the safety mission matters to him at all? You've spoken to him a few times. I think when he talks about still caring about and prioritizing safety—look, maybe I am just kind of too generous in my assumptions about people—I assume that he believes the story that he is telling himself, which very often has the same contour. Namely, what he cares about is winning; that's the word he uses. And he has set up the rationales in his mind and in the way he conveys it to others, so that him winning and consolidating as much power as possible is the same thing as humanity winning. So for him, when he talks about these things, there is no daylight between them. You know, he says in the piece that his definition of winning is a crazy up-level for everyone. You know, we're all gonna be curing diseases and forming blockbuster startups—and he really, I mean, really paraphrasing, but barely exaggerating. And sometimes that's unavoidable, but sometimes people can use it to their rhetorical advantage. Right? So on questions of safety or alignment, what you would often find is that the Sam Altman of 2015, or even frankly 2023, would be talking about the alignment problem as a literally existential problem: "If we don't solve this, a rogue AI could kill literally every person on earth." And then you go back to him two years later and he's saying, "The alignment problem is this slightly annoying thing that kind of tempts you to, you know, spend more time on ChatGPT the way that Instagram's algorithm is out of alignment with the way you want to spend your time." That is absolutely not what he meant by it two years prior, but the word is the same, so he can kind of escape by with that. Yeah, I think actually that's a very important point, Ed. I think your analysis is correct and actually the perspective from which we write the piece is, these have always been shifting assurances. And actually part of what we emphasize and document is that even at the time of those older assurances where Sam Altman was the biggest doomer in the industry, he was saying and doing conflicting things at the same time in many cases. You know, we document through a lot of internal communications a period of time in which many researchers were joining the company on the promise that this would forever be a noble non-profit, but simultaneously Sam Altman and Greg Brockman, his co-founder, were having very concerted conversations about how quickly and effectively they could get it out of the non-profit structure essentially. We talk about cases where he's saying on the Hill, "We need regulation, you know, we need to start even a new government agency to regulate AI," and then simultaneously he's working against regulations. Well, just to bolster the point you're making, like, what really does seem clear is that whether they should have believed it in retrospect, a lot of these early employees, early recruits, early co-founders really do seem to believe it. It is true that private companies sometimes keep outside investigations when they want the outcome to be exonerative out of writing, you know, to limit liability and because of privilege issues. So final question, actually in line with that, do you think Sam Altman sees himself as a businessman or as a—as a statesman almost? That's a really interesting question. I think that, you know, he—I honestly sense in my conversations with him that he sees himself as something quite singular. And the grandiosity of his claims about his mission, you know, when he was telling recruits that he believed that this technology could eventually—what was the exact quote Andrew, "capture the light cone of all the value in the universe"? I think he exhibits a trait that I have seen writing about various Silicon Valley moguls of this era, which is they—they do have a kind of messianic quality to how they see themselves. You know, not as businessmen, not as statesmen, but as everything to everyone, as supergovernmental. I think Elon is a much more extreme example of that psychologically—the kind of messiah complex, there's much more mania there. Sam is someone who—as evidenced by the fact that he participated in this story so deeply—still wants to kind of play by the rules and within a system to some extent. But the reality around all of these guys is we have let Silicon Valley grab all of the levers of power in the United States. It is such a center of gravity economically, it is—I mean, one could say more or less the entire economy at this point, and it is bankrolling a huge, huge swath of politics now. So it is very hard I think for the people at the forefront of that to consider themselves anything but messianic. And you add to that the specific contour of AI in which these guys really believe, and not without reason, that they are birthing the future of the Earth, that this may supersede the human species as the next dominant thing—I think that really magnifies the messianic quality. And so that all goes to the responsibility question. Like yes, to Andrew's point, you know, business people, they fib and they dissemble and they are guilty of empty hype and that is the whole saga of Silicon Valley from the beginning. The fact that Sam Altman is not in my view, you know, a villain in any clear-cut way, and the piece goes to pains to be generous to him, to me makes this a more interesting story because both things can be true. You can have someone who is, you know, not a monster, but who is still in a position of so much power that if they have this particular trait where no one can trust them and what they say is going to happen with the most dangerous technology on Earth might not be what happens—that is something that we should all care about. I think it just speaks to this wider set of structural issues where this is the most acute need we have as a species for proper governance and regulation and that is all falling away and it's all in the hands of these private unaccountable individuals. Well, thank you so much, Andrew, Ronan, for joining me, this has been an awesome conversation. The piece will be linked—and yeah, thank you for joining me. Thank you so much, Ed. What's God got to do with it? The case for faith in an age of AI

World Economic Forum(1.1M) ·39:09 ·641 views · 2026-04-09
> "There are two forces that have shaped the history of the world, and those are commerce and faith.
TL;DR (1-2 sentences): Faith communities represent over 80% of the global population, making them essential partners for addressing global challenges in climate, economics, and AI by grounding technical solutions in long-standing moral frameworks and human-centric values. ...

TL;DR (1-2 sentences): Faith communities represent over 80% of the global population, making them essential partners for addressing global challenges in climate, economics, and AI by grounding technical solutions in long-standing moral frameworks and human-centric values.

The Strategic Necessity of Engaging Faith

  • Massive Global Scale: Approximately 80-85% of the world’s population identifies with a religion or faith tradition. To achieve goals in finance, climate, or technology, global leaders must know how to cooperate with these massive communities.
  • Reuniting Commerce and Values: Historically, commerce and faith were deeply interlinked. Gopal Patel (FutureFaith) notes that they have become "delinked," and the goal is to reintroduce spiritual and moral considerations into economic and technical systems.
  • Mitigating Polarization: By normalizing faith in professional and global contexts, leaders can prevent religion from being "co-opted by the extremes," which often leads to division and conflict.
  • Institutional Infrastructure: Faith organizations possess vast, existing infrastructure—including hospitals, schools, and financial networks—that can be mobilized for the common good.

Building a "Moral Economy"

  • Addressing Wealth Inequality: The current economic system is failing many and causing environmental damage. The Council explores what a "moral economy" looks like—one that serves both people and the planet rather than rewarding greed.
  • Equitable Resource Distribution: Drawing on Gandhi’s teaching that there is enough for "need but not for greed," faith leaders advocate for systems where capital flows toward human flourishing rather than simple accumulation.
  • The "Toolbox" Approach: Soulaima Gourani (Happioh) explains that the Council is developing a "toolbox" for leaders and boards to ensure their policies and technologies are aligned with universal faith-based ethics.

Environmental Stewardship and Climate Action

  • Depoliticizing Climate Language: Because the phrase "climate change" has become politically charged and alienating, faith leaders aim to use language rooted in values and self-interest to mobilize the majority.
  • Translating Ancient Wisdom: Every major religion has roots in nature (mountains, deserts, forests). The challenge is translating that 2,000-year-old wisdom into a modern urban context (e.g., London or Nairobi) to address the current crisis.
  • A Legacy of Leadership: Faith groups have spent decades working on biodiversity and conservation; the Council seeks to catalyze this momentum through partnerships with the private sector.

The Human Side of Artificial Intelligence

  • Research Acceleration: Soulaima Gourani highlights that AI can compress 10 years of rare disease research into 10 days, offering immense potential for human longevity and healthcare.
  • The Soul vs. The Algorithm: While AI can mimic personality, it lacks an "inner life" or soul. Faith provides a necessary framework for navigating the "human fallout" of AI, such as the grief and loss of identity associated with mass job displacement.
  • Guarding Against Misuse: Tech leaders are increasingly open about their faith to ensure they build products that improve human lives rather than facilitating "evil" purposes, such as surveillance or social isolation.
  • Non-Transactional Relationships: While AI can provide "all-knowing" answers, Gopal Patel argues it cannot replace the "revelation, surrender, and devotion" found in a spiritual relationship, which evolves as a human grows in consciousness.

Advice for Global Leaders

  • Challenge Modern Assumptions: Many global systems are built on Judeo-Christian assumptions that may no longer work or may never have worked in certain parts of the world.
  • Embrace Change: Using Hindu philosophy, Patel suggests that when a system is "breaking," it is often a sign that something "new and beautiful" is coming. Leaders should not fear the end of old systems but work to usher in the new based on spirit and values.

At an immature stage or a young stage, we get very simple answers, but as we grow in our love and devotion to God and with God, the answers become much more refined or much more nuanced or much more sublime. And that's something that AI can't replace or take away. Why The OpenAI Legal Battle Could Redefine The Future Of Artificial Intelligence

Forbes(2.1M) ·13:24 ·8.6K views · 2026-04-09
Elon Musk’s $134 billion lawsuit against OpenAI, set for trial in late April, represents a fundamental clash over whether the future of Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) should remain a nonprofit public good or a high-capital for-profit enterprise.
TL;DR Elon Musk’s $134 billion lawsuit against OpenAI, set for trial in late April, represents a fundamental clash over whether the future of Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) should remain a nonprofit public good or a high-capital for-profit enterprise. Trial Timeline:...

TL;DR

Elon Musk’s $134 billion lawsuit against OpenAI, set for trial in late April, represents a fundamental clash over whether the future of Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) should remain a nonprofit public good or a high-capital for-profit enterprise.

The Musk vs. OpenAI Legal Battle

  • Trial Timeline: Jury selection is scheduled to begin on April 27th, marking a critical escalation in the feud between Elon Musk and Sam Altman.
  • Core Allegations: Musk claims Altman and OpenAI leadership "unjustly enriched" themselves by transitioning from a nonprofit to a for-profit structure, violating the company's founding mission.
  • OpenAI’s Counter-Defense: Executives allege Musk’s legal action is motivated by "ego" and bitterness over his failed 2018 attempt to take over the company and merge it with Tesla.
  • Financial Remedies: While the suit seeks $134 billion, Musk's legal team recently clarified that he is not seeking personal gain; instead, he requests that damages be awarded to OpenAI’s nonprofit foundation to restore its original mission.

IPO Pressures and Market Competition

  • SpaceX and xAI Merger: Elon Musk’s companies, SpaceX and xAI, reportedly filed confidentially to go public on April 1st, with a potential IPO expected as early as June.
  • OpenAI’s Public Aspirations: OpenAI is rushing toward its own IPO, potentially later this year. Phoebe Liu notes that if Musk succeeds in forcing a reversion to a nonprofit structure, it would be "much harder to accomplish" once the company is publicly traded.
  • The "Capital Race": Current AI leaders (OpenAI, Anthropic) are winning primarily because they have the most capital.
      • Burn Rate: These companies are generating billions in revenue but losing even more due to the massive computing costs required to train and run models.
      • Funding Dependency: OpenAI likely needs the public markets’ equity and debt funding to continue its current trajectory.

Allegations of Anti-Competitive Behavior

  • Regulatory Requests: OpenAI has sent letters to officials in California and Delaware requesting investigations into Musk for "anti-competitive behavior" ahead of the trial.
  • The Zuckerberg Connection: OpenAI alleges that Musk and Meta CEO Mark Zuckerberg have coordinated "opposition research" and attacks against Sam Altman to undermine OpenAI’s market position.
  • Failed Smear Campaigns: Reports suggest that extensive opposition research into Altman’s conduct—aimed at painting him as prioritizing profit over safety—has yielded "close to nothing" that could be substantiated or published.

The Future of AGI Development

  • Nonprofit vs. For-Profit Trajectory:
      • A nonprofit OpenAI would be obligated to release models publicly, potentially slowing the current "AI revolution" but democratizing access.
      • A for-profit OpenAI continues to keep its most powerful models closed to maximize revenue and attract the billions in investment needed to compete with Google’s DeepMind.
  • Succession Planning: Amid the turmoil, Sam Altman has reportedly joked about an "AI CEO" taking over OpenAI in the future, signaling that he may not intend to lead the company indefinitely after it goes public.
"The companies that are leading the AI race right now are the companies that have the most capital... [OpenAI is] making billions of dollars in revenue per month and losing even more." — Phoebe Liu
"Elon did not want any of the 134 billion to be for his personal gain. He wanted to put it in the nonprofit's bank... that dismantles a lot of the claims that he just wants this to be rich." — Phoebe Liu
"If [OpenAI reverts to a nonprofit], that will change how the world is thinking about AI because this is the most well-capitalized for-profit now AI company in the world." — Phoebe Liu

Advancing to AI's Next Frontier: Insights From Jeff Dean and Bill Dally

NVIDIA Developer(206.0K) ·59:02 ·691 views · 2026-04-09
AI is transitioning from supervised task-completion to autonomous "agentic" workflows, necessitating a radical hardware shift toward ultra-low latency inference and AI-assisted chip design to sustain the next frontier of performance.
TL;DR AI is transitioning from supervised task-completion to autonomous "agentic" workflows, necessitating a radical hardware shift toward ultra-low latency inference and AI-assisted chip design to sustain the next frontier of performance. Performance in Verifiable Domains:...

TL;DR

AI is transitioning from supervised task-completion to autonomous "agentic" workflows, necessitating a radical hardware shift toward ultra-low latency inference and AI-assisted chip design to sustain the next frontier of performance.

The Evolution of AI Models and Autonomous Agents

  • Performance in Verifiable Domains: Models have seen massive leaps in math and coding. Jeff Dean highlighted Google’s Gemini model achieving gold-medal status in the International Mathematical Olympiad (IMO) and ICPC coding contests.
  • Transition to Agentic Workflows: AI is moving from interactive prompting to long-running "agents" that can execute tasks independently for hours or days, correcting their own errors without constant human supervision.
  • Natural Language Research (NAS):
      • Researchers can now specify search spaces in natural language rather than code (e.g., "Explore new distillation algorithms").
      • Agents act as a productivity multiplier by running experiments, dismissing unproductive leads, and honing in on promising architectures.
  • Data and Scaling: Dean disputes the idea that "data is mined out," pointing to untapped video/audio data and the potential for high-quality synthetic data generated by powerful models.

Hardware Architectures for Ultra-Low Latency

  • The Latency Goal: NVIDIA aims to drive inference performance from hundreds to tens of thousands of tokens per second (10k–20k range) to support reasoning-heavy agent workflows.
  • Communication Bottlenecks:
      • On-Chip: Using static scheduling to eliminate routing overhead and queuing, aiming for "speed of light" data transfer (e.g., across a chip in 30 nanoseconds).
      • Off-Chip: Reducing Physical Layer (PHY) latency by simplifying signal processing. Dally suggests backing off bandwidth slightly to eliminate the latency-heavy "heroic" error correction and digital signal processing.
  • Provisioning Bifurcation: The industry is moving toward specialized hardware SKUs for different tasks:
      • Training and Prefill: Math-heavy and communication-dominated.
      • Decode: Memory-bandwidth limited and highly sequential.

Efficiency Through "Zero Movement" and Advanced Memory

  • The Energy Cost of Data: Reading a number from external memory (HBM) costs approximately 15 picojoules, whereas the actual computation (multiply-add) costs only 10 femtojoules.
      • Core Philosophy: "The key thing you can do to reduce energy is to just don't move data."
  • SRAM vs. DRAM: While SRAM is energy-efficient for computation, its area cost is an order of magnitude higher than DRAM.
  • Stackable DRAM: NVIDIA is exploring stacking DRAM mats directly on top of the GPU logic. This could provide an order of magnitude more bandwidth with an order of magnitude less energy by eliminating the distance bits must travel to the pins.
  • Challenges of Sparsity: While models are naturally sparse, exploiting that sparsity often destroys the regularity that makes hardware computation efficient, requiring more complex controllers.

AI-Driven Chip Design (AI for AI)

  • Automating Library Porting: NVIDIA’s NVCell uses reinforcement learning to port standard cell libraries to new semiconductor processes overnight—a task that previously took a team 80 person-months.
  • PrefixRL: Reinforcement learning has been used to design circuit adders that are 20–30% smaller and lower power than those designed by humans.
  • LLMs for Engineering (ChipNeMo):
      • NVIDIA fine-tuned internal LLMs on proprietary RTL code and design documents.
      • ChipNeMo acts as a "patient mentor," explaining complex texture unit architectures to junior designers and summarizing bug reports.
  • The Future of Design: The ultimate goal is an end-to-end agentic system where a "Master Agent" coordinates specialized sub-agents to handle floor planning, verification, and timing, collapsing the design-to-tape-out timeline.

Societal Impact and Personalized AI

  • Education: Dean and Dally emphasize the potential for personalized AI tutors that understand an individual's specific knowledge gaps and learning modalities (e.g., turning a biology chapter into an interactive game).
  • Healthcare: The "Personal Health Coach" concept—an AI that monitors biometric data (watches, genomics) and provides real-time dietary and medical advice, potentially bridging the gap between clinical data and patient behavior.
  • Scientific Discovery: Using AI to accelerate the identification of new materials and susceptibility to diseases based on personalized mutations.
"The key thing you can do to reduce energy is to just don't move data... people are laughing but I'm serious. That's absolutely what you have to do." — Bill Dally
"It used to be you could ask your model to do some stuff... but now you can actually give these models tasks that take hours or perhaps even days and they will go off and independently do a bunch of things, correct themselves, and do more things." — Jeff Dean
"[Reinforcement Learning] comes up with totally bizarre designs that no human would ever come up with but they're actually 20 or 30% better than the human designs." — Bill Dally
GTC enthusiasts, please welcome to the stage Google Chief Scientist Jeff Dean and NVIDIA Chief Scientist Bill Dally. So Jeff, it's been an exciting year for machine learning. What's sort of the most exciting development you've seen in the past year in machine learning? What do you expect to see next year also? Yeah, I mean I think everyone in this field has seen, you know, really rapid progress in what these models can do and what the capabilities are and also how people are applying them. So that's been really interesting and exciting. You know, I think for the last year or so, I would say reflect on a couple of different things. One is I feel like the models have gotten way, way better at sort of problems with verifiable rewards, so things like math and coding. And, you know, a few, three or four years ago, we were super excited when our models could, you know, solve eighth-grade math problems. You know, Fred has four rabbits and gets two more rabbits, and then they would get that, you know, 40 or 50% of the time. And that was—everyone was like, yeah, that's great. But, you know, in the last few years and particularly in the last year, we've really accelerated how effectively these models can do mathematics. And so you saw like our Gemini model entered the IMO contest and got a gold medal. You know, in coding contests, the ICPC contest, we also got a gold medal there. So I think, you know, the capabilities in those domains are really impressive. And then maybe a little bit even more recently than that, we started to see really agent-based workflows work extremely well for longer-running kinds of things. It used to be you could ask your model to do some stuff and it would go off and do it and a few minutes later, you'd have to now say, "okay, great, it finished that, and what's the next thing it can do?" But now you can actually give these models, you know, tasks that take, you know, hours or perhaps even days and they will go off and independently kind of do a bunch of things, correct themselves, do some more things. And I think that's a pretty exciting, you know, transition because now it leads to the ability to have these models kind of operate fairly autonomously for longer periods and that I think is a a very different thing. Whereas before you'd kind of have to sit there, not quite interactively but supervising them fairly closely. So that seems like a pretty exciting change. I mean speaking of this, it seems like we're going to have a lot more of these agents kind of operating in the background and that one of the things that's really going to be important is how do we get ultra-low latency inference so that these systems can, you know, do their work autonomously, you know, faster, because I think that's a really rapid driver of how effectively they can solve problems. How do you see the next architectures you at NVIDIA are working on delivering, you know, major latency reductions? How do we think about getting to, you know, instead of hundreds of tokens per second to thousands or tens of thousands? Yeah, I actually I talked about this in my talk at 10 o'clock this morning, which I think was recorded if you missed it. And and there are a bunch of answers to that question. You know, when you look at the sort of performance curve for, you know, inference, it's a tradeoff between, you know, at one extreme if you're willing to give up latency you can get very high throughput and in sort of tokens per second per dollar or tokens per second per watt. And then you can move down that curve and basically running smaller batch sizes you can optimize more for interactivity, more, you know, single-user tokens per second at the expense of of that throughput. And as you get down to the, you know, the right side of that curve where you're really optimizing for latency, it turns out that the bulk of the delay is communication latency. And and so, you know, we're working on, you know, developing architectures where we drive that latency down, you know, to what we in NVIDIA always refer to as speed of light. And if you look at a typical, you know, LLM where you're doing a bunch of feed-forward, you know, stages, then a bunch of attention stages, and that's one layer and you do that for, you know, anywhere between 50 and a couple hundred layers. Every time you finish one of those stages there's usually an on-chip communication to get to the next stage. And then after you finish a layer, it's often an off-chip communication to get to the next layer and sometimes even between stages within a layer depending on how you've partitioned the problem. And on-chip, you know, we've got, you know, trial designs whereby statically scheduling things so that there's no routing overhead, there's no queuing, there's no arbitration. We basically can achieve, you know, 2 millimeters per nanosecond, basically the time of flight over over wires on the chip and get from one corner of the chip to another corner of the chip in 30 nanoseconds. Does that really drive the latency down from what was several hundred nanoseconds today? And then off-chip, a lot of the latency is actually in our PHY because for years we have optimized these PHYs for the maximum bandwidth without too much consideration for latency. And so to push the extremes, you know, we wind up with heroic digital signal processing to pull this super noisy signal off the wire and run all sorts of fancy math to figure out what the bit is, and even then the bit's kind of noisy so then we do heroic forward error correction to to pull the right bit out. And it turns out if you back off in the bandwidth just a little bit, you know, go from say 400 gigabits per second per pair to 200, all of a sudden all that goes away. You can just detect what the voltage on the wire, you get the bit, and it's just serialization latency at that point, it's a couple clock cycles going on and off chip. So we see our way to build whole routers, you know, similar to the routers I built at Cray 20 years ago in 2006 when we did the BlackWidow; the router was less than 50 nanoseconds, you know, pin to pin. I think we can do that again and by doing that I can see us running, you know, relatively big models, at, you know, 10 to 20,000 tokens per second per user. Yeah, I mean that's super exciting. I think this is a it's really important to be able to run the largest scale models at these scales, at these low latencies. Okay. So I'm a big believer in the use of AI for various aspects of chip design. Both, you know, if you think about the chip design phases, there's like high-level architectural exploration, there's more automated synthesis of high-level designs into lower-level things, there's automating verification, and then there's automating placement and layout and routing. And so we I've done some work on AlphaChip, as one example, helping with placement and routing. Very great Nature paper, by the way. Oh, thank you, thank you. And, how do you think—and I've seen some really nice work from NVIDIA on various kinds of gradient-based methods for placement and routing. So how do you think about the use of AI in the actual hardware design process? Do you think like tackling these different stages with different AI-based approaches is the right thing? Do you think a much more end-to-end thing is possible? How do you see this evolving? So we're trying to use AI wherever we can in our design process. And there are a number of examples. So for example every time we have a new semiconductor process, we have to port our standard cell library to it. It's about, you know, 2,500-3,000 cells. And that used to take a team of eight people about 10 months, so 80 person-months. And then we developed a, you know, a program based on reinforcement learning called NVCell, I think we're up to NVCell 2 or 3 now, and it's overnight on one GPU and the results are actually better than the human designs. It in measures of goodness of size of the cells, power dissipation, delay, it it matches or exceeds exceeds the human designs. So that's a huge productivity gain and and it it eliminates an obstacle to moving to new processes because now we can move move cell libraries over very quickly. And then we have this program called PrefixRL, which applies reinforcement learning to a really age-old problem in computer design, which is where to put the look-ahead stages in a carry look-ahead chain. This is a problem that's been studied since the 1950s and, you know, this RL, you know, program goes at it like it was an Atari video game and it you know puts something in here and it grades itself and it's not trying to make the fastest adder, it's trying to make actually the adder that barely meets timing and is as small and low-power as possible. It comes up with totally bizarre designs that no human would ever come up with but they're actually, you know, 20 or 30% better than the human designs by by those metrics. And then at a more holistic stage, we've for a number of years had had a series of LLMs that we've called ChipNeMo and BugNeMo. And what we did is we took a, you know, a generic LLM and then we, fine-tuned it by feeding it all of the design documents proprietary to NVIDIA. So this is stuff that you can't get outside the company, it's all of the RTL— Hardware design docs and proprietary stuff. Yeah, all of the RTL for every GPU ever designed at NVIDIA, all of the architecture specs for those, all sorts of stuff. And now you have this LLM that's actually very smart about GPU design. And and one of the biggest gains of this is when you have a junior designer, it turns out the senior designers spend an enormous amount of their time explaining to the junior designers simple things like, you know, "How does the texture unit work? In particularly this texture unit." And now they don't have to ask the senior designers that, they can ask ChipNeMo that and ChipNeMo will explain to them in great detail how the texture unit works, they can have a follow-up question it'll answer the follow-up question. So it improves productivity that way. It's a very patient mentor. Yeah, yeah. And and, you know, the same thing when we have bugs, we can have it summarize bug reports and try to do attribution on bugs, say, "Which module is this bug in? Who do we ARB?" We always ARB somebody for a bug, "Action Required By", and so we'll decide, you know, what designer gets ARB'd for for a particular bug. And then, you know, we're at the more exploration level and particularly in NVIDIA Research, we're now using agentic systems to try to, you know, do a bunch of thought experiments where we say, "Okay, here's a bunch of different ways we could go, you know, figure out what this parameter space looks like, suggest new ways of doing things, run simple architecture experiments and whittle the design space down to a smaller number of things that that we can look at." And, you know, we're trying to collapse, you know, the time. You know, at NVIDIA there's a stage when we're kind of done with the exploration effort, that we call we have the F-model, it's basically an executable model of of the GPU, to where we tape out the design. We have all the geometry done, it goes out to TSMC to make chips. And we we'd like to collapse that space. And what turns out what the real long pole in that space is is design verification. And so we're particularly looking at how we can use AI to, you know, prove the designs work more quickly. There are also a couple stages in there where we have to refactor the design because we have, you know, the RTL design is done, but then we've re-partitioned things for floor planning. So to hit that, you know, 10,000 plus tokens per second regime for reasoning-heavy agent workloads, you know, which algorithmic techniques, you know, advanced speculative decoding, MoE routing at massive scale, ultra-low precision inference, do you think will require the biggest corresponding hardware changes? Like deeper support for per-vector quantization, optimal clipping, or logarithmic representations? Yeah, I mean I think to really make low-latency inference effective for very large models, you have to think from first principles about what what can we do to minimize the data movement, minimize the energy, and minimize the time to go from, you know, an input to the output of a block or a layer, attention calculation, or whatever. And those things really are, you know, probably about rethinking how that entire hardware pipeline looks, not about a particular, you know, numeric format here or there. I think it really requires kind of rethinking quite a lot about the inference hardware because really you don't want anything to move. I mean some people laughed when we said that earlier, but I I think that's really the key is don't move anything and it'll be super efficient and super low energy. So you could do that if you put everything in SRAM, but then it becomes super expensive. Yeah, yeah, so, you know, new memory technologies are always welcome, I would say. You know, I think the stackable DRAM on top of a chip is probably a pretty interesting direction because then you can get much more capacity and cheaper cost per bit and still have the—you don't move the data very much. It goes up and down. It's like a pachinko machine where you just drop the drop the bit you want down into the operator. So I think those are those are probably really exciting directions as how do you make incredibly large models, you know, very low latency. Because that I think will improve the whole use of agents and all the—assuming we make our tools fast enough as well. That will really, you know, improve the energy usage of these systems. Yeah, and to put the numbers straight, typically, you know, doing a multiply add say for NVFP4 is like 10 femtojoules. And to pull those four and a half bits in, you know, from, you know, HBM4 at order of, you know, maybe three or four picojoules per bit, you know, you're talking about, you know, 15 picojoules. So it's a thousand times more energy to read one NVFP4 number from memory than it is from external memory than it is to do a multiply add. But reading a, you know, simple SRAM bank is also on the order of about 10 femtojoules. And so, you know, the the key thing you can do to reduce energy is to just don't move data. And, and, you know, people are laughing but I'm serious. That's absolutely what you have to do. And so, you know, for example we're looking at at designs where, you know, for where we can put things in SRAM, we have an SRAM, we can read a, you know, row of a matrix out of that SRAM, have a, you know, a row of a activation vector, and do a dot product right there. And and so we've done two things: we've done basically, you know, say a thousand multiply adds, with no data movement, we just, you know, read the SRAM, everything is there in place, we do a thousand multiply adds— Read from your left hand, read from your right hand, put it down. That's right, yeah, and then we've reduced the data from being a thousand long to one by by doing the dot product. And so we're trying to look at a lot of techniques like that. And then the problem then is that SRAM is expensive. It in terms of, you know, area area per bit, it's an order of magnitude more expensive than DRAM. And the technology we're really excited about but it's not quite ready is where we can stack the DRAM on top of the chip that's doing the computation and then we can, you know, basically cycle the DRAM mat as they call it, and sense the bit and bring it straight down. It turns out most of the read energy of reading the DRAM isn't actually reading the DRAM, it's moving the bit from where you read the DRAM over to where the pins to the GPU are. And so by putting this DRAM directly on top of the GPU, we can get an order of magnitude more bandwidth with an order of magnitude less energy per bit, which winds up being the same power but with, you know, way way more performance. And those are really the things we're looking at. Of course more efficient models will help also. I mean if you can get the same accuracy with less work, that reduces energy also. And the one place there that I think that can really help a lot is with sparsity. We've already done a lot with sparsity starting with Ampere with the, you know, two-to-one structured sparsity, but these models are just very naturally sparse. But it's also the case that other than the two-to-one structured sparsity and the very coarse sparsity of MoE, it's hard to exploit sparsity in a way that wins. Because once you start exploiting it in a more general sense, you create a lot of irregularity, or I should say you destroy the regularity and the regularity is a lot of what makes a computation efficient. Because you get all the bits kind of marching along in in regular order. When you disrupt that, you then need to do much more, you know, controller-oriented and data routing-oriented things to deal with an irregular nature. So I think it's really kind of an interesting open question how to push to the next level on sparsity. Yeah, definitely. I mean I think models that kind of reuse their parameters for many times are kind of nice in some some way. Reuse is good but reuse is is again it's part of that curve trading off, you know, optimum throughput, optimum tokens per watt against optimum interactivity. And when you're really worried about interactivity or when you're only running a single model, and you've got one token it's got to see every weight, the reuse is one. Yeah, unless it's like a convolutional model in which case you get quite a bit of reuse across the the convolution. But for LLMs, it's a matrix-vector. So one of the things about our TPU designs is they have custom networks that tend to be, you know, 2D or 3D toruses. sort of kind of point-to-point things, but they scale to very large numbers of chips, you know, thousands of chips. And that is a different approach than say a sort of fully switchable network fabric that enables you to scale to you know, larger than just a machine worth of chips. How do you think about the tradeoffs in these two designs and... Well that's a great question, you realize that, you know, one of my major academic research areas was interconnection networks and I literally wrote the book on this subject. And it turns out that, you know, 2 and 3D torus networks are really great for a lot of things, in fact, you know, I kind of led a revolution in the, you know, late 1980s early 90s, to well have a whole series of supercomputers designed with almost all 3D torus networks, the Cray T3D being the sort of the big example. And then into the 2000s what happened is we got so much pin bandwidth on chips that that when you're building a router chip you couldn't really effectively use it if you only had six connections, which is what you get with a, you know, three-dimensional bidirectional network. And so we we moved into this area where we built what we call high-radix routers and we moved to a whole series of new topologies like, you know, folded Clos networks which are often called fat trees and dragonfly networks and things like that. And it really for, you know, for picking, you know, you can't say one is better than the other because it really has to do with what is the workload and what is the traffic pattern. If you have a very local workload, a direct connected network with with relatively low radix like like a 3D torus is really ideal because you can map your problem. So part of the problem is on on this, you know, TPU and part of the problem is on this TPU and you have to go one hop to get your data over there. But then other cases where for example you have an MoE model and you've got a lot of experts and they're scattered all over the place and now you're having to take many hops to get to a given expert. And so it would be more efficient to do one hop up to a switch and one hop down to the expert that you really want to be at. You know, when we've played around with some of these tradeoffs we've often come up with the hybrid one. We had one, you know, sort of experimental inference system that will never see the light of day that we we did in NV Research where the the local communication to allow things right next to each other to communicate was, you know, a direct connection, no switches, but with a flattened butterfly topology. So you had a little bit more reach than you have with a with a torus. And then all those chips were also connected to a switch and and all the more global communication was done, you know, through through a hierarchy of switches. Yeah, I mean I totally agree. It depends on exactly what your workload is and so given a traffic pattern you can come up with an optimum network, but there's no network that is good for all traffic patterns. Yes, indeed. So as we, you know, as we build these wonderful machines that run wonderful models that that, you know, do things that become even more amazing every day, what do you think is going to be the most positive impact on sort of human existence out of this? Is it in education or medicine or business productivity? What what are you most excited about the fruits of our labors improving for humankind? Yeah, I mean I think this is a really good question. I think there's obviously a whole range of things that AI can be applied to and some are incredibly societally beneficial, you know, education, healthcare. ChatGPT and Cancer: How a Tech Founder Rewrote His Treatment Plan

OpenAI(1.9M) ·48:27 ·5.6K views · 2026-04-09
Sid Sijbrandij (Co-founder, GitLab) Jacob Stern (Geneticist)
After receiving a terminal diagnosis for a rare and aggressive bone cancer, GitLab co-founder Sid Sijbrandij utilized "founder mode" and advanced AI tools to move from zero treatment options to over 30 personalized therapies, successfully achieving "...
TL;DR After receiving a terminal diagnosis for a rare and aggressive bone cancer, GitLab co-founder Sid Sijbrandij utilized "founder mode" and advanced AI tools to move from zero treatment options to over 30 personalized therapies, successfully achieving "no evidence of disease"...

TL;DR

After receiving a terminal diagnosis for a rare and aggressive bone cancer, GitLab co-founder Sid Sijbrandij utilized "founder mode" and advanced AI tools to move from zero treatment options to over 30 personalized therapies, successfully achieving "no evidence of disease" through a data-driven, engineering-led approach.

"Founder Mode" and Maximal Diagnostics

  • Data-Over-Protocol Strategy: When standard care failed, Sijbrandij transitioned to a "maximal diagnostics" approach, collecting 25 terabytes of personal biological data (available at osteosarc.com) rather than waiting for specific clinical indications.
  • Single-Cell Sequencing: This technology allowed the team to isolate individual cancer cells, leading to the discovery of high FAP (fibroblast activation protein) expression.
      • This discovery enabled a targeted radioactive treatment in Germany that resulted in 60% tumor necrosis and 20% shrinkage.
  • Single-Patient IND Pathway: Sijbrandij utilized the FDA’s "single-patient Investigational New Drug" pathway, which allows terminally ill patients access to experimental treatments outside of traditional large-scale trials.

The Role of AI in Personalized Medicine

  • Democratizing Specialized Knowledge: Jacob Stern (Geneticist) noted that AI functions as an "Iron Man suit," allowing non-specialists to navigate arcane medical literature, formulate hypotheses, and communicate effectively with world-class experts.
  • Automated Bioinformatics: The team built AI agent systems to:
      • Perform automated literature searches and structure bioinformatics analyses.
      • Analyze over 600,000 single cells from Sijbrandij’s blood to rule out side effects like leukemia (CHIP).
      • Execute complex Python code for data visualization at a fraction of the cost ($20 in API fees) and time (30 minutes) of traditional methods.
  • Identifying Hidden Targets: AI identified PANX3 as a primary target for Sid’s cancer—a protein represented 10,000x more in his cancer than healthy tissue.
      • Humans had overlooked PANX3 because it is hydrophobic; AI’s ability to scour massive datasets without human "patience" constraints allowed for the discovery.

Engineering Custom Biological Treatments

  • Personalized mRNA Vaccines: Sijbrandij became the first patient in an investigator-initiated trial using a vaccine encoded with his specific cancer mutations. AI was used to select which antigens to include in the limited "bits and bytes" of the vaccine.
  • CAR-T Cell Logic Gates: To prevent a "nuclear bomb" treatment from destroying healthy organs, the team engineered a "logic gate" (AND gate) for his CAR-T therapy.
      • The treatment requires both B7H3 and FAP to be present before activating, protecting his liver, which showed high expression of only one of the markers.
  • TCR-T Cell Therapy: Using sequencing data, the team identified T-cell receptors specific to Sid’s cancer and engineered them into healthy T-cells to be kept "on the shelf" as an insurance policy.

Scaling the "Roadster" to the "Model 3"

  • Shifting Incentives: Sijbrandij argues that the medical system is designed to minimize liability, whereas patients need to maximize survivability. He advocates for trying multiple treatments in parallel rather than sequentially.
  • Paving the Road for Others: To make this approach accessible to the general public, the team has launched/supported several ventures:
      • Velios: Focuses on maximal diagnostics to find targets for any cancer regardless of tissue origin.
      • Arden: Uses AI to parse massive (9,000+ page) medical histories to treat complex immune disorders.
  • Systemic Advocacy: Sijbrandij is pushing for "Clinical Trial Abundance" by:
      • Reforming "Institutional Review Boards" (IRBs) to reduce administrative delays.
      • Adopting "notification-based" trial approvals (similar to the Australian model) to lower the $1 billion+ cost of bringing drugs to market.

Notable Quotes

"I came to the conclusion that I’d rather die from a treatment than from the cancer. Dying from cancer is a really miserable way to go. That is not what you will get with doctors; they have very different incentives than you." — Sid Sijbrandij
"AI does feel like an Iron Man suit to allow us to assess all these different specialty things... it makes it accessible to somebody like me." — Jacob Stern
"We’re trying to bring the standard of care 30 years from now into the present... for Sid and a few others, we are starting from scratch, but we want to pave the road behind us so it’s easier for the next person." — Jacob Stern
"AGI may be the tide that lifts all boats, but many of us are particularly excited about how it can transform medicine... the future is very bright." — Scott McKinney

It affects fewer than 1,000 Americans a year, and if it recurs after the first line of treatment, survival rates are typically measured in months. I myself have been fighting the same fight since 2020, and I've seen firsthand how sparse, blunt, and frankly gruesome the treatment options are, and I've seen how quickly they can be exhausted.
